1. East Java Water Supply Project






I.  THE SECTOR
A. Background
1.01      Population.  Indonesia is the fifth most populous country in the
world with more than 150 million people 'Living on 3,000 islands. The
average population density is 77 per sq km, but in East Java (the Province
in which the proposed Project is located') it is 609, one of the highest in
the world. With 30 million people, the province has 20% of Indonesia's
population and 2% of its land area. Indonesia's population is increasing at
an average annual rate of 2.3% and in Java the rate is 1.5%. About 20% of
the national population lives in urban areas which are growing at 3.7% per
annum. Eleven percent of East Java's population reside in its eight self-
governing cities (Kotamadya). The largest of these, Surabaya, has 2.2
million people and is, after Jakarta, Indonesia's second largest city.
Tables 1 and 2 of Annex 1 provide additional data on population, population
density and land area for Indonesia, Java and East Java Province.
1.02      Administration.  Indonesia has 27 provinces which are divided into
216 districts (Kabupatens), and these in turn are divided into about 3,300
subdistricts (Kecamatens).  There are 54 autonomous cities (Kotamadyas),
about 3,300 district towns (Ibu Kota Kecamatens), and about 65,000 villages
(Desas). East Java Province has 29 Kabupatens, 54 Kecamatens, 8 Kotamadyas
and 8,260 Desas.
1.03      Health.  As measured by standard indices, health conditions in
Indonesia lag behind those in other countries at a similar stage of economic
development. At the end of the 1970s, life expectancy in Indonesia was 53
years, while the average for other low- and middle-income countries was 57
and 61 years, respectively. The average infant mortality rate was estimated
at 93 per 1,000 births compared to about 55 in the Philippines and Thailand,
and 32 in Malaysia. Infant mortality rates as high as 200 per 1,000 births
have been reported in some areas. Endemic diseases, caused principally by
poor water supply and sanitation, are major factors behind these statistics.
In 1974, the latest year for which comprehensive statistics are available,
Indonesia exceeded all other countries in the number of cases and deaths
from cholera. Additional comparative health indicators, together with other
basic indicators, are given in Annex 1, Table 3.
1.04      Water Resources.  Indonesia is well endowed with rainfall and
ground and surface water resources, but problems of distance, reliability,
contamination, excessive demand and conflicting uses preclude simple and
rapid development on a wide scale. Because of population density, intensive
agriculture and above average industrialization and urbanization, the water
supply situation in Java is more critical than in other parts of Indonesia.
Although Java has above average rainfall, (a) it has only 6% of the nation's
available water; (b) its per capita water availability is only 9% of the



national average and (c) the estimated 1990 demand represents 98% of the
available supply in Java and 46% of the nations total demand.  For further
details, see Annex 1, Table 4.
1.05       Indonesia water resource specialists estimate that household and
industrial water requirements are about 5% and agricultural needs about 95%
of total needs. Much of household needs can be supplied from subsurface
water and springs, but usually the location and reliability of such sources
are not precisely known and their development requires exploration time and
expense. Surface water, while more readily accessible, has the disadvantage
of requiring more specialized treatment which is both cosl:ly to build and to
operate. On balance, from technical and economic standpoints, subsurface
and spring sources are preferable and all of the schemes under this Project
use these sources.
B. Sector Service Levels
1.06       About 40% of the urban population had access to piped water supply
in 1981. Much of this suffers from contamination, low pressure, and
intermittent supply. A considerable portion of the urban population does
not have access to public water supply and is dependent on water from
vendors or from shallow wells, streams or rivers at considerable health
risks.  Only 20% of the urban population has satisfactory sanitation
facilities.
1.07       Of the rural population, only 18% had access to potable water from
either piped systems, wells with handpumps, central rainwater collectors or
protected springs. The remaining 82% obtain water from unprotected streams,
springs or wells. Only 24% of the rural population is estimated to have
satisfactory sanitation facilities.
1.08       While the present situation is unsatisfactory, there has been
considerable improvement in water supply over the period 1970-81 during
which the percentage of population served rose from 2.5% to 23%. However,
the absolute number of people without access to safe water remained almost
the same at 118 million in 1970 as against 117 million in 1981. The
proportion of the population served by sanitary facilities in the country
has increased from about 15% in 1970 to 23% in 1981, but the population not
served also increased, i.e., from 103 million to 117 million, with the urban
areas particularly falling behind. The water supply and sanitation
situation in 1970 and 1981, in both the urban and rural areas, is shown in
Table 1.1.

C. Sector Institutions
1.09      Three ministries of the Government of Indonesia (GOI) are
principally responsible for the water supply, sewerage and sanitation sector.
The Ministry of Public Works is responsible for development in urban areas,
the Ministry of Health for rural areas, and the Ministry of Home Affairs,
indirectly, for overseeing operations (see para. 1.24). Other government
agencies with a role in the sector include the National Development Planning
Board (BAPPENAS) which allocates funds for development projects, and the
Ministry of Finance which disburses funds to the executing agencies and
establishes the terms under which such funds are provided.
1.10      Within the Ministry of Public Works, the Directorate of Sanitary
Engineering (DSE) of the Directorate General Cipta Karya (CK) is responsible
for overall planning, design and implementation of water supply, sewerage
and sanitation projects funded by GOI (see para. 2.02). During the period
of construction DSE and CK provide technical and managerial assistance to
water supply and sewerage utilities and on occasion temporarily operate water
utilities until they become semi-autonomous water enterprises (PDAMs). At
present they operate about 80 such utilities.
1.11      The Ministry of Health, through its Directorate of Hygiene and
Sanitation (DHS) is responsible for rural water supply and sanitation. DHS
supervises design and construction, and assists in operation and maintenance.
CK provides technical advice to DHS and the public works departments of
individual provinces and kabupatens undertake construction.



- 4 -
1.12       Water and sanitation systems are owned and operated by local
governments, either by a department (a Dinas), or by a semi-autonomous public
enterprise (a PDAM). Occasionally these entities also engage in the
construction of projects. The Ministry of Home Affairs, by virtue of its
responsibility for local government and local government enterprises, oversees
such operations.
D. Sector Development
1.13       During the period covered by GOI's First Five-Year Development Plan
(REPELITA I, covering 1969-74), total capital investment in the sector was
close to Rp 16 billion. About 80% of this amount was contributed by GOI, and
included the funds provided by external assistance agencies; the balance was
obtained from various local sources including provincial budgets. The initial
emphasis was on water supply; production capacity increased from 9,000 liters
per second (lps) to 15,000 lps during the period. Investment during REPELITA
II (1974-79) amounted to Rp 166 billion, of which Rp 140 billion was invested
in urban projects and Rp 26 billion in rural projects. Productive capacity
increased from 15,000 lps to 21,000 lps during this period.
1.14       In 1979 as REPELITA II Twas drawing to a close, it was recognized
that the pace of development needed to be accelerated, and that in particular
the smaller cities and towns and the poor of those places must be accorded
priority. Therefore, during REPELITA III (1979-1984) the Government embarked
upon a "basic needs" approach to projects which seeks to rapidly provide
essential service at low-cost for the cities and towns included in the current
and successive Plans. Under the impetus of the UN International Drinking
Water and Sanitation Decade overall service level targets were set by GOI in
1981 for 1990 as follows: urban and rural water supply to serve 75% and 60%
of the total population, respectively; and urban and rural sanitation to serve
60% and 40% of the total population, respectively. Two major programs for
meeting the targets for urban water supply in the 1980s are the Basic Needs
Approach (BNA) program for cities and larger towns (with populations 20,000
and up), and the Ibu Kota Kecamaten (IKK) program for kecamaten headquarter
towns with populations in the 3,000-20,000 range (para. 1.17).
E. Earlier Bank Lending
1.15       The Bank has made two urban water supply loans which cover a total
of 12 cities. The first, (Loan 1049 of 1974) sought to apply the principal
recommendations of a 1971 sector report that (a) financing for projects be by
loan rather than grant; (b) semi-autonomous water utilities (PDAMs) be estab-
lished and developed; (c) DSE be reorganized and its management capabilities
improved; (d) the functions of the key ministries active in the sector be
defined; and (e) staff be trained to implement projects and operate systems.
That project is now virtually complete but experienced a 2-1/2 year delay
caused by poor performance of the consultant responsible for design, slow and
cumbersome procurement procedures, and delayed deliveries by several
suppliers. PDAMs for the beneficiary cities have been developed and are
generally performing satisfactorily. DSE, which underwent reorganization and



- 5 -
staff strengthening, improved its capabilities but is still overburdened
because its workload expanded so greatly (see para. 2.02). Important
departures from covenants under the loan agreement concern: (i) the
Government's failure to submit adequate audited financial statements for the
beneficiary PDAMs (Section 4.02(b) of Loan Agreement 1049); and (ii) the
failure of Government agencies to pay their water bills (Section 4.05(b) of
Loan Agreement 1049). A problem on this first project is in its delivery of
benefits: the installation of service connections for three of the five PDAMs
has been delayed and the expected benefits from them will accrue more
gradually - from one to three years later - than originally planned. The
delay resulted from project implementor's late recognition that connections
were a part of the main project framework and not, as had been past practice,
a part of the PDAM-s routine connection program. The Project Completion
Report currently under preparation will review these and other issues.
1.16      The second Bank project (Loan 1709 of 1979) followed the lines of
the first project. It continued the strengthening of DSE, the establishment
and development of seven PDAMs, and the training of staff. It experienced
delays in preparation and procurement of about one year but now all supply
contracts have been placed and it is moving at a normal rate. Audited
financial statements and payment of bills by Government agencies are also a
problems under this project, as they are country-wide. Audits are the subject
of ongoing dialogue with Government. In addition to the establishment in 1931
of an Accounting Development Board to advise the Government on development of
accounting and audit, present discussions are focusing on the establishment of
a Governmental Commission with powers to execute major improvements of
accounting and auditing in Indonesia. For PDAMs receiving support under Bank
projects, the Directorate General of State Financial Control of the Ministry
of Finance is designated as independent auditor. During negotiations CK
confirmed that it will employ independent GOI-authorized accountants from the
private sector to assist the PDAMs in p:reparing their accounts for audit.
Regarding nonpayment of water bills by GOI agencies, CK agreed to (i) study
the underlying the causes for non-payment; (ii) provide assistance and
training to PDAMs in the areas of billing and collecting; and (iii) mount a
campaign to increase public awareness of the cost of (and hence the need to
conserve and pay for) potable water.
F. Urban Water Supply - Policies, Issues and Constraints
1.17      Government Objectives and Mechanisms.  The Government-s urban water
supply objective as enunciated at its 1982 National Workshop /1 is to provide
by 1990 water supply systems to 75% of the urban population. It would do this
by continuing BNA and IKK Programs (para. 1.14) started in Repelita III, that
is, to build systems with grant financing which provide an average of 60 lpcd
(45 lpcd for IKK) to about 60% of the population. Systems with capacities
from 60 to 120 lpcd would be financed by a blend of loans and equity, and
above 120 lpcd by loans only. Government-s program includes training of water
/1 Hleld in Bali in October 1982 and attended by representatives of BAPPENAS
and the Ministries of Finance, Health, Home Affairs and Public Works.



-6-
supply staff, public health education, and improving the capabilities of local
consultants, civil works contractors and suppliers.
1.18      BNA and IKK projects are distinguished from earlier ones in that
special attention is given to speedy implementation, low cost construction,
and designs which focus on household needs including the poor. The intent
is to build systems which provide an essential service at an affordable
price, to use scarce manpower effectively , and to demonstrate to consumers
the benefits of safe water so the community itself will take initiatives to
extend and improve the service. Accordingly facilities are of low cost
design; design procedures are standardized; half of the population served
receives house or yard connections, the other half standpipe service;
projects are executed in the provinces and kabuputens by local staff to the
extent capabilities there permit; goods are purchased in bulk and
distributed from regional and central stores; the projects are financed as
grants and are given free to the communities; and tariffs are expected to
cover operation and maintenance costs and the depreciation, (although some
subprojects may be subsidized in the early years of operations when there are
few connections).
1.19      Government-s basic needs programs have praiseworthy objectives and
the mechanisms for implementating them are logical. The Bank-s strategy for
the sector is to support the Government's program, to help identify areas
where improvements are desirable, and, over time, to assist in bringing
about such improvements. The ADB and the Australian and Netherlands
Governments are equally committed to support the sector and there have been
good coordination and useful information exchanges between these and other
external agencies and the Bank. The proposed Project follows current
practices but encourages and supports reassessments which in the longer term
should further strengthen the sector./l These reassessments would be in the
areas discussed below.
1.20      Investment Funds and Cost Recovery.  The investment required to
meet the 1990 urban water supply target is estimated to be about US$1.7
billion, or US$240 million per year for the remaining years of the decade.
This represents a three fold increase over the 1981 sector investment budget
and an allocation to urban water supply of more than one percent of the
total development budget. Given Governments stringent resource position,
it is considering options to recover from water users a portion of the
capital costs, which would contribute to a revolving fund to be used to
offset the capital cost of subsequent projects. Analysis by Bank staff of
subprojects in East Java province indicate that such cost recovery could
finance a portion -- perhaps up to 30% -- of the capital cost with tariffs
at levels which can be afforded by East Javan communities. Under the
/1 In addition to the dialogue which took place during the preparation of
this Project the Bank has under way an Urban Sector Study which is
analyzing institutional and financial arrangements for urban develop-
ment generally. The results of its work will complemernt that being
done in connection with water supply projects.



-7-
proposed loan, consultants will study the merits, effects and institutional
arrangements for capital recovery, and the findings of the study would help
shape policy in this area (para. 6.06).
1.21      Project Implementation.  By law the provision of water supply is
the responsibility of local government, but in reality the central govern-
ment plays the leading role in design, construction and funding of projects.
At the first National Workshop for Water Supply and Sanitation (Bali, 1981)
the participating ministries agreed to promote regional autonomy in develop-
ing projects. This is desirable given the size and geographical dispersion
of Indonesia, and because it builds up Local capabilities, shortens the
management span and helps ensure that projects are responsive to local
needs. Consistent with the Workshop's recommendations, recently more design,
planning and construction supervision work is being done in the regions.
But it is usually done by a province-based office of the central Directorate
of Sanitary Engineering rather than by offices of the provincial government;
hence not all of the advantages of decentralization are achieved. Under
this Project, as described in para. 4.01, there will be more local
responsibility than heretofore and the arrangements will serve as a test for
future projects in East Java and other provinces.
1.22      Operation and Maintenance.  Water systems, although generally
built by DSE, are operated by the local government either as a unit of its
public works department (a Dinas) or as a local government enterprise (a
PDAM). As a Dinas, staff and accounts are not kept separate from other
government functions and water revenues flow into the local government's
general fund. As a PDAM, the enterprise is directed by a board, it employs
management and staff who work exclusively for the utility, and accounts are
independent of other agencies. The concept of PDAM's is sound and Govern-
ment intends that eventually all urban water systems will be operated by
them.
1.23      The functioning of PDAMs is not without problems.  Newly employed
staff require extensive training and experience, and accounting, management
and operational systems have to be developed. Overstaffing is common and
some local governments have used the water systems as a source of income,
(resulting in excessive connection charges and underutilized capacity).
These practices frequently continue despite the intent of financial and
managerial autonomy. Each PDAM has a Board of Directors with representation
from local and central governments, but the notion of assertively providing
a public service through a financially viable, well managed organization has
to be instilled virtually case by case. Advice and control by a higher
authority also appears to be necessary.
1.24      As part of its institution building function, on centrally funded
projects DSE sets up PDAM management anid accounting systems and trains staff
in their use (and assurances to this effect, for the PDAMs benefitting



- 8 -
from the Project, have been obtained at negotiations). But after projects are
complete DSE has few legal powers and insufficient staff to continue control.
The Directorate for Urban Development of the Ministry of Home Affairs nominal-
ly has the authority to supervise and assist the PDAMs but it has neither
staff nor qualifications to do so. In any case the numbers and diversity of
PDAMs are so great that in the long run, central controls over individual
PDAMs probably would not be workable. In provinces which have numerous
PDAMs (such as East Java) a provincially based control unit would appear to
be appropriate, possibly located as a part of an existing office of the
Provincial Government, and, through a line of command, responsible to the
Governor. Under the proposed Project DSE will provide assistance during the
construction period and the Bureau for Promotion of Economic Infra-
structure /1 of the East Java Provincial Government would gradually assume
responsibilities as the facilities come into operation. The Bureau among
other things, would: (i) provide technical and financial guidance to the
PDAMs; (ii) supervise the operation, finance and management of PDAMs; (iii)
review and assist in the preparation of tariff and other charges; (iv) study
investment proposals and review their priority and justification; (v)
provide channels to exchange information on the improvement of systems and
procedures. These are new functions for the Bureau; the functions would be
prescribed in detail, and Bureau staff trained, by consultants employed
under the Project.
1.25      Human Resources for the Urban Water Program.  The Directorate of
Sanitary Engineering has in progress a manpower development program which
was started in 1979 under Dutch assistance. The program is one (among many)
of the Ministry of Public Works training activities. It has the objective
of training management, administrative and operating personnel for DSE and
water enterprises. The program has experienced difficulties, some due to
design, some to implementation, and at present is not operal-ing at a
satisfactory level. Cipta Karya, with the participation of consultants and
a Bank training specialist, is re-evaluating and reforming it, and
attempting to move more of the training activities to the provinces. Under
the proposed Project, funds are allocated to support, in parallel with Dutch
funds, a revitalization of both regional and national train:ing activities;
(details are in Annex 2.) This would complement a broader effort to
strengthen MPW staff training activities to be supported by the proposed
Manpower Development Project, and DSE would utilize as appropriate the
facilities and services provided under that project. The form and function
of the training program to be supported by the proposed loan were confirmed
at technical discussions which preceeded negotiations. Details are in
Annex 2.
/1  The Indonesian designation is Biro Bina Pengembangan Sarana
Perekonomian Daerah.



- 9 -
II. THE BORROWER AND) BENEFICIARIES
A. The Borrower
2.01      The Government of Indonesia normally acts as the borrower of loans
provided by multilateral and bilateral agencies. It then passes the
proceeds, together with the local currency requirements, to the executing
agencies. For the proposed project, GOI would be the borrower and the
proceeds would be allocated to Cipta Kary'a - the proposed executing agency
which, in cooperation with the East Java Provincial Government and eight
kabupatens, would implement the Project. On completion of the Project the
assets would be turned over to the eight PDAMs as equity and management and
operations would be entrusted to them.
B. Cipta Karya and DSE
2.02      Cipta Karya, with a staff of about 5,000, is composed of five
Directorates, i.e., Building Development, Building Research, Housing, City
and Regional Planning, and Sanitary Engineering. The Directorate of
Sanitary Engineering (DSE), with a staff of about 1,600, is composed of the
subdirectorates of Planning, Construction, Development and Logistics and an
Administration Division. DSE's Planning Center (under the Planning
Subdirectorate) is the focal point for the planning and implementation of
BNA and IKK projects. Its 350 engineers and technicians, with the assist-
ance of consultants, prepare projects, train regional staff in project
design, review designs, and monitor projects and programs. DSE also has
regional project offices in 20 of the 27 provinces; these offices normally
execute projects outside Jakarta. The East Java project office is staffed
with 12 engineers and technicians and annually executes about 20 water
supply projects. For large projects and those which receive foreign
assistance DSE appoints specific project mnanagers who work under the general
supervision of the Director of DSE. The staffing of DSE is shown in Annex 1
Table 5 and the organizations of Cipta Karya and DSE are shown in Charts 1
and 2.
2.03      About 500 of DSE-s staff are employed at the headquarters in
Jakarta. Of the total central staff about 300 are directly engaged on
central planning, development and construction activities. About 30% of the
staff at the headquarters hold engineering or technical degrees and another
25% are educated in finance or administraltion. DSE's staff virtually
doubled over the last several years, in parallel with the growth in sector
programs. But despite this increase, there are still manpower constraints
in terms of numbers of people, their educational qualifications and
experience. For this reason DSE is making increasing use of its province-
based offices, and cooperates with provincial, kotamadya and kabupaten
governments in the implementation of projects.



- 10 -
2.04      Cipta Karya and DSE receive considerable technical assistance: the
Bank is financing the services of a full-time expatriate Management Advisor;
the Australian Government has provided six engineering advisors and is plan-
ning additional technical assistance projects; WHO provides a full-time
sanitary engineering advisor; UNDP is funding a sanitation specialist; WHO
and DANIDA are helping to develop a monitoring and information system; and
the Dutch Government provides engineering consultants and, until 1982, an
economist and an engineer to assist the Planning Division of Cipta Karya's
Secretariat.
C. The Provincial and Kabupaten Governments
2.05      East Java Province is managed by the Governor who, while reporting
to the Ministry of Home Affairs, is also the representative of all central
ministries active in the Province. In 1982 the Provincial Public Works
Department of East Java was split into three departments, Cipta Karya (for
urban development), Bina Marga (roads) and Pengarin (irrigation), each of
which reports to the Governor. The new Provincial Cipta Karya, which has
taken over the urban development staff of the former Provincial Public Works
Department, will share responsibility for project implementation with
Central Cipta Karya. To this end, in addition to its own staff, it would
use qualified persons from the public works departments of the eight second
level governments (i.e., seven kabupatens and one kotamadya) which are
beneficiaries of the Project. The public works capabilities of the
provinces of Java are generally recognized as among the best in Indonesia.
D. Water Enterprises - PDAMs
2.06      Project facilities would be owned and operated by PDAMs under the
direction and ownership of the kabupaten or kotamadya governments in which
they are located. Law No. 5 of 1962, Law No. 5 of 1974, and the Ministry of
Home Affairs Decree 26 of 1975 provide the legal framework for PDAMs. There
is considerable experience in establishing and operating PDAMs in Indonesia./l
The regulation for each PDAM is approved by the respective People's Council
of the kotamadya or kabupaten and it is ratified by the Governor. Whereas
typically PDAMs were organized to serve a single urban area, in order to
effectively and efficiently use management systems and manpower, PDAMs have
recently been organized to operate all urban systems within a kabupaten.
Such is the model for the PDAMs covered by the Project, a typical organiza-
tion is shown in Chart 3. Under the proposed Project, five PDAMs are
already formed and operate limited existing systems and three others would
be formed before the systems come into operation (para. 6.01). The
Preliminary Design Studies (Document B.1 in the Project File) describe the
existing PDAMs and their proposed organization. PDAM management reports to
a board of directors and the board chairman is typically the head (Bupati)
of the kabupaten. The board appoints the Managing Director who, with the
/1 Twelve PDAMs are beneficiaries under two earlier Bank loans. They have
been established and strengthened by technical assistance components of
the projects, and many of the management systems developed for them are
now used nationwide.



- 11 -
assistance of three deputy managers (Finance, Technical and Operations), is
responsible for implementing policy and conducting day to day business.
Kabupaten PDAMs have a head office in the principal city and local operating
units in the other cities and towns of the kabupaten. Tariff adjustments
are approved by each PDAM and ratified by the Bupati and Governor. There
are -national guidelines for the tariff structure (para. 6.04) which are
generally followed by PDAMs.
III. THE PROJECT /1
A. General
3.01      In the late 1970s, the Government developed its Basic Needs Approach
(BNA) for water supply with the objective of providing low cost essential
service to numerous cities and towns with populations of 20,000 or more
(para. 1.14). As a part of this program, CK employed consultants to prepare
25 such projects in East Java. Subsequently Government added the IKK program
to its development efforts. The original plan was to do two projects in East
Java with Bank support, one under each program. But in 1982 these were merged
as a single project because many of the subprojects from the two programs were
in the same kabupatens and would be operated by the same PDAMs, thereby
enabling administrative and physical efficiencies. Accordingly, this Project
is comprised of subprojects in 13 cities and 37 IKK towns, one located in a
Kotamadya (Madiun) and the others in seven of East Java-s 27 kabupatens.
3.02      The beneficiary cities and towns were selected by CK in consultation
with the Ministry of Home Affairs, the East Java Provincial government and
local governments using a rating and ranking system which included: cost
(favoring towns easily supplied by groundwater or springs), need (where there
is a significant water service deficit or health problem), economic growth
potential, and parallelism with other government programs. The ranking
system was reviewed by Bank staff and is satisfactory. The original list
included 25 cities and 100 towns scattered throughout East Java Province. In
order to keep the project manageable, and so that attention could focus on the
/1  The analysis presented in this Appraisal Report is based on an exchange
rate of 700 Rupiah to the US dollar. However, after project appraisal and
loan negotiations the Government announced on March 30, 1983, the devalua-
tion of the Rupiah from 700 to 970 per dollar. While it is difficult at
this early stage to completely assess the effect of this decision we have
concluded that it will not materially change the basic scope and objec-
tives of the project. The base cost of the project as expressed in
dollars equivalent, would decline somewhat and consequently the rate of
Bank participation in the project would increase slightly. The project
cost, as expressed in Rupiah will increase both because of the 38% upward
adjustment of the Rupiah equivalent of the foreign costs and an increase
in local inflation which can be expected to follow the devaluation
decision. Consequently, tariffs about 7% higher (in 1983 prices) than
indicated in the appraisal will be needed to comply in 1988 with the
revenue covenant. This would not however affect the affordability of the
project to the beneficiaries since the resulting higher water bills would
be well within the capacity of even the poorest consumers to pay.



- 12 -
development of kabupaten - wide PDAMs, 50 cities and towns located in eight
kabupatens were selected for an initial project. Government intends to carry
out a subsequent project for additional cities and towns in the near future.
3.03       The subprojects in the BNA cities were prepared by Sir M. Macdonald
and Partners (acting as lead consultant) and three consortia of Indonesian
consulting companies (totaling 13 firms). The IKK subprojects were prepared
by P.T. Ciriajasa and Associates, a consortia of Indonesian firms. All
preparation work was funded by proceeds of Bank loan 1709, the Second Water
Supply Project. Several missions visited Indonesia over the period 1979-1982
in connection with the Project. There were several policy issues which were
addressed during the course of project preparation and this process took time
and effort. These included (a) tariff and cost recovery issues (para. 1.20);
(b) arrangements for project implementation (para. 1.21); and (c) institu-
tional issues such as how PDAMs would be controlled (para. 1.24).
3.04       The Project is based upon preliminary design reports prepared by the
consultants which cover engineering, institutional, operational and financial
subjects. These reports, completed in September-October 1982, are the primary
documents for this appraisal. Some concepts and assumptions were subsequently
modified, most notably in the area of demand and financial projections
(paras. 5.03 and 6.09).
B. Objectives
3.05       The objectives of the Project are to:
(a) provide potable water to about 60% of the population and
commercial and industrial users in 50 cities and towns of East
Java./l The Project is expected to serve about 720,000 persons.
About half of them are poor and most would have access to potable
water for the first time;
(b) establish PDAMs, develop or improve their organizational
capabilities and strengthen their technical and financial
performance;
(c) continue Bank assistance to DSE and Cipta Karya and strengthen
their managerial capabilities;
(d) establish a permanent training unit for East Java Province,
strengthen DSE's capability to manage nationwide training, and
assist in the more rapid decentralization of training to other
provinces; and
(e) provide a vehicle for study and dialogue to promote efficient and
appropriate policies and institutions, including financial policies,
/1 In order to simplify project preparation and implementation the Govern-
ment has asked external agencies to concentrate their efforts on
designated provinces. Thus ADB supports projects in Central Java and
South Sumatera; the Netherlands, West Java, North Surmatera and Aceh; the
Bank, East Java and, in the future, the three provinces of Sulawesi.



- 13 -
technical criteria and institutional arrangements, for the water
supply sector.
C. Content
3.06      The Project consists of 50 water supply subprojects, technical
assistance for project implementation and institutional development, and
training. The water works component consists of fifty piped water supply
systems. Each system will be complete with source development, pumps and
motors where necessary, transmission and distribution pipe, service reservoirs
and treatment where appropriate, service connections and public standpipes,
and office and workshop space (a description of each system is in the Project
File).  Water sources would be either spring or tubewells since these have
been determined to be lowest cost and capable of producing water of sufficient
quantity and quality./l Gravity pressure systems will be used where feasible
to avoid the need for pumping equipment and recurrent energy costs. Pipes
will be of polybutylene, polyvinylchloride or asbestos cement, reservoirs of
reinforced concrete. Service connections for BNA subprojects would be
metered, for IKK subprojects, fitted with flow restrictors (see para. 3.07).
(Flow restricted service connections enable the distribution system to be of
lower capacity and hence of lower costs. They also eliminate meters and
costly volume-based billing. Their disadvantage is that the consumer cannot
instantaneously draw a large volume of water, thereby necessitating in-house
storage.) Water from all systems would be chlorinated and meet WHO Drinking
Water Standards; most systems would meet these standards without treatment
(for those few systems where treatment is required, only aeration is neces-
sary). The estimated numbers of these facilities are given in para. 3.10.
Sanitation is not included in the Project but is being addressed under other
government programs, including those of INPRES and Kampung Improvement
Program (KIP),/2 and government will coordinate those programs with water
supply to the extent feasible.
3.07      Planning and Design Criteria.  Thirteen of the subprojects are in
cities which fall under the Government-s BNA program, and 37 in towns under
the IKK program (see paras. 1.14, 1.17 and 1.18). Although the numbers and
locations of the cities and towns are established, the option remains to
substitute a limited number of them provided their replacements meet the
selection criteria and funds are sufficient. There are eight existing systems
in the BNA cities; five will be abandoned because of age and inefficiencies,
and three will be integrated with the proposed subprojects. There are no

Consequently groundwater exploitation is coordinated with irrigation
activities such as those funded by the Bank-s Seventeenth Irrigation
Project.
/2  INPRES is a presidential funding source which is provided directly to
provincial and kabupaten projects of their choice. KIP is a program to
improve the quality of crowded urban areas; typically it covers walks,
roads, drains, water supply and sanitation. KIP is financed in part by
the Bank-s urban development loans.



- 14 -
existing systems in the ITKK towns. Facilities are designed in accordance with
DSE criteria, standards and procedures which have been developed with the
assistance of consultants. The design service levels and how the water is
allocated is shown in Table 3.1. Improvements in design criteria have
evolved over the past year and this would continue over the course of the
Project. Also systems presently being installed would be monitored (in
particular those with flow-restricted service connections, see para. 3.06)
with the view of improving the effectiveness of future design and ensuring
customer acceptance. At negotiations assurances were obtained that DSE,
under terms agreed with the Bank, would arrange for a review of design
criteria and monitor systems in operation. Design and physical works under
this Project are proceeding without awaiting the results of this review but
recommendations of the review would be adopted for this project where
feasible, and for future projects. Annex 4 presents details of the planning
and design criteria, lists items to be included in the above review, and would
serve as a supervision checklist for the review of final designs and draft
contracts.

3.08      The institutional development component would (a) build the eight
beneficiary PDAMs into effective, financially viable water enterprises; (b)
train staff and revitalize the water supply training program at the national
and regional levels (para. 1.25) and develop guiding principles for the
organization of PDAMs generally; (c) study, recommend and install offices,



- 15 -
systems and arrangements for assisting and monitoring PDAMs over the long term
(para. 1.26); (d) study PDAM tariffs and the merits and arrangements for cost
recovery (para. 6.06); (e) review and improve planning and design criteria
(para. 3.06); (f) continue assistance to DSE and CK so they may more
effectively carry out their responsibilities under the Government's expanding
urban development and water supply programs. Consultants would assist DSE's
Development Subdirectorate in carrying out (a) and (b) above and would
assist CK in (c), (d) and (e). For (f), management consultants would be
employed to make an assessment of DSE's and CK's responsibilities,
functions, development targets, management systems, organization, staffing
and operating budget, and recommend improvement actions. Annex 3 elaborates
on the technical assistance and study components of the Project.
3.09      Engineering services under the Project consists of assistance to the
Project Management and Implementing Units i;n project management, construction
supervision and manufacturing plant inspections. These services would be
provided by local and foreign consultants. Additionally consultants would
be employed to prepare future water supply projects.
3.10      Summarized, the Project elements are:
(a) Civil Works and Equipment
(i) 1,400 km of pipe and 3,500 valves of 20 mm to 400 mm;
(ii) 50 forwarding pumps of 5 to 40 liters per second capacity;
(iii) 70 tubewells with screens, casings, pumps and motors;
(iv)  80 chlorinators;
(v) 15 reservoirs of 60 to 1,000 cu m capacity;
(vi) 86 diesel generators of 5 to 40 Kw output;
(vii) 63,000 service connections and 45,000 water meters; and
(viii) miscellaneous supporting equipment, office buildings, ware-
houses and works.
(b)  Institutional Development and Training.  The provision of
experts and consultants to develop PDAMs, establish a training
system and train trainers, develop capability for assisting and
monitoring PDAMs, review design criteria and improve the effec-
tiveness of CK, including DSE.
(c) Engineering Services. The provision of consultants for project
management and supervision, review of design criteria and the
preparation of future projects.



- 16 -
D. Cost Estimates /1
3.11      The total cost of the Project, including physical and price
contingencies, is estimated at Rp35.6 billion equivalent to US$50.9 million,
including a foreign exchange cost of US$27.5 million equivalent, or
about 54% of the total cost. Taxes and duties are estimated at $0.5 mil-
lion equivalent. An abridged summary of the cost estimates is provided in
Table 3.2, and detailed Project cost estimates are provided in Annex 5.
3.12      The cost estimates for the physical works are based upon prelim-
inary designs for nine of the 13 BNA, and all of the IKK subprojects. Costs
are based upon information obtained from manufacturers, Iocal contractors
and suppliers, bid results of recent projects in East Java, and published
rates of various governmental agencies. The base costs are expressed in
January 1983 prices.  Physical contingencies have been estimated at 15%.
Price contingencies for local costs were estimated at 13% for 1983, 10% for
1984 and 7% per annum thereafter, and for foreign costs at 8% for 1983, 71/2%
for 1984 7% for 1985 and 6% thereafter.
3.13      The cost of consultants for project supervision, technical
assistance and training is estimated at US$5.6 million equivalent. This is
comprised of US$2.0 million equivalent for engineering and supervision
(about 7% of the cost of physical works), US$2.7 million for technical
assistance and US$0.9 million for the preparation of future projects. The
cost of consultants are based on 1,072 and 312 man-months of local and
foreign personnel, using an average total cost (including travel, subsis-
tence and overhead) per man-month of US$2,500 equivalent for local and
US$10,000 equivalent for foreign personnel.
E. Project Financing
3.14      A Bank loan to GOI of US$30.6 million equivalent would finance the
foreign exchange cost of the project, the capitalized front-end fee of about
US$0.08 million, and about $3.06 million in local cost. The Bank loan will
finance 60% of the total cost, excluding reserve procurement for vehicles
and motorcycles. The loan would be for a period of 20 years, including a
grace period of 5 years, at the standard variable rate of interest applied by
the Bank. GOI would provide the balance of Rp 14.2 billion ($20.4 million
equivalent) from its own resources as DIP funds (i.e., development budget
funds) to Cipta Karya by way of annual budgetary appropriations. Bank loan
and DIP funds will be provided as grants to the PDAMs in accordance with
Government policy. This policy is accepted for this Project but will be
reviewed under the cost recovery and financial study funded under the Project
(paras. 1.20 and 6.06). The funds needed in Rp billion are 0.20 in 1983, 5.22
in 1984, 6.52 in 1985, 1.70 in 1986 and 0.60 in 1987 (Table 3.2). Given the
normal time required to obtain reimbursement for withdrawal applications,
actual budgetary requirements of CK would be somewhat higher in the first
year. The financing plan is shown in Table 3.3.

IV. IMPLEMENTATION ARRANiGEMENTS
A. Project Management
4.01      The implementation arrangements for the Project follow a recently
adopted standardized model. The Directorate General of Cipta Karya, through
its Directorate of Sanitary Engineering (DSE), will have overall responsi-
bility for the Project and for international procurement. A Project
Management Unit (PMU), to be a part of the East Java office of the Ministry
of Public Works (Kantor Wilaya P.U.) and assisted by the East Java Office of
DSE, will manage and coordinate project activities. It will be responsible
for selecting sites, preparing designs and procurement documents, procuring
those goods and civil works covered under local procedures, supervising
construction, and coordinating all other project activities including assis-
tance to PDAMs studies and training (the latter of which will be provided by
DSE's East Java office). The staff of the PMU have been designated by CK
and the Provincial Government. Day-to-day implementation will be handled by
a Project Implementation Unit (PIU) working under the overall supervision of
PMU. The PIU will be headed by a project manager (an engineer has already
been designated), and will supervise subproject managers each of whom will
be responsible for a group of subprojects within a kotamadya or kabupaten.
The PMU and PIU will draw staff from Provincial Cipta Karya, the subproject
managers will be designated by the beneficiary PDAMs or kabupaten public
works office. (The establishment and staffing of both units would be a
condition of effectiveness.) The PMU and PIU will be assisted by consultants
for design, construction supervision and management.  The Ministry of Rome
Affairs, through its Directorate of Urban Development and Regional



- 19 -
Autonomy, will assist on policy matters and in dealings with the provincial
and local governments. Table 4.1 and Chart 4 depict these arrangements.
4.02      On December 29, 1982 the Provincial and Central Government entered
into an agreement which sets forth their respective obligations with regard
to (a) implementation of the physical, institutional and training aspects of
the Project; (b) establishing the Project Implementation Unit and providing
it with necessary staff and other resources; (c) ensuring that the
beneficiary kabupatens are provided support in fulfilling their obligations
for project implementation and establishing and developing PDAMs; and (d)
participating in the studies concerning the tariffs and monitoring of PDAMs
(paras. 1.24 and 6.11, and Item 2.C of Project File).
B. Implementation Schedule
4.03      Procurement actions for goods under the Project began in October
1982 when the Bank approved a first draft document to purchase pipes, meters
and service connections. This first contract, which is a critical path item
for pipelaying, was advertised in January 1983 and first deliveries are
expected in the second quarter of 1984 after which pipelaying will begin
with the following exception. At negotiations CK stated that it intends to
expedite some works by using selected materials on hand. These meet the
technical specifications approved under the Project, and would be replaced
subsequently by materials procured under the Project. Civil works (such as
buildings and reservoirs) which are not dependent upon separately procured
goods or which will incorporate material on hand will begin in mid-1983./l
Initial works (which would enable the delivery of some water service) would
be completed in the last half of 1984, and all work would be complete by
1987.  Chart 5 shows the project implementation activities and schedule and
Annex 6 describes the overall implementation program.
C. Project Supervision
4.04      The main responsibility for project supervision will necessarily
rest with Government, because of the numerous project activities and records,
and because physical works are numerous, widely scattered and inaccessible.
Experience with the first water supply project implemented by DSE indicate
the need to strengthen the project monitoring and supervision function at
DSE headquarters, and for this (among other) purpose a resident management
advisor was appointed under the second water supply loan. Because of the
increasing numbers of projects in recent years, it has become apparent that
supervision must be decentralized from Jakarta. For this Project the PMU
has been dLesignated by GOI to be responsible for supervision. To this end
it will issue semiannual reports (to be copied to the Bank) which would
contain, among other things:

D. Procurement
4.05      Civil works to be implemented under the Project estimated at
US$16 million excluding contingencies would be widely dispersed, labor
intensive, of small scale and therefore not suitable for ICB. IKK civil
works contracts would average less than US$100,000 each, BNA contracts, less
than US$500,000 although about two contracts would may be slightly more than
US$1 million. Therefore civil works would be bid in accordance with GOI
procurement regulations using standardized documents which are satisfactory
to the Bank.  They would be packaged to enable small contractors to partici-
cipate individually, and to attract large local contractors or joint
ventures of smaller firms to bid for groups of contracts.
4.06      Equipment and materials with an estimated value of US$14.8 million
(excluding contingencies) to be procured under the Project include pipes,
fittings, well screens, casings, pumps, meters and chlorination and well
drilling equipment. Vehicles including motorcycles will be subject to
reserve procurement because Government restricts their importation.
Equipment and materials required in small lots or on an urgent basis, valued
individually at less than $100,000 and totalling less than US$2.0 million,
would be procured through GOI procedures acceptable to the Bank. All
remaining equipment and supply would be procured under ICB. Local
manufacturers would be granted a preference of 15% of the CIT price of
imported goods, or the customs duty, which ever is lower.



- 22 -
4.07      Contract Review.  No prior review of civil works procurement
documentation by the Bank is proposed because: (a) all civil works
contracts are individually small and will be let on thes basis of local
procedures; and (b) Bank emphasis has been to review and agree on the local
procedures to be followed and the standard contract documents to be used;
not much is to be gained through contract by contract reviews. Bank
supervision missions will, however, conduct random post review. All bidding
packages for equipment and materials over US$200,000 equivalent would be
subject to the Bank's prior review of procurement documentation.  This would
cover about 95% of the total value of goods.
E. Disbursements
4.08      Disbursements would be made at the rate of 100% of the foreign
expenditures for directly imported goods, 95% of local expenditures (ex-
factory) for goods manufactured locally and procured under international
competitive bidding, and 65% of local expenditures for imported goods which
are procured under local procedures. Disbursements for consultant services,
training specialists, and overseas training would be at: 100% of
expenditures. Disbursements for civil works contracts would be at 45% of
cost on the basis of periodic statements of expenditures submitted by GOI.
There would be no disbursements for motorcycles and vehicles. The proposed
allocation of loan proceeds is given in Annex 7, Table 1. The loan would be
disbursed over the period December 1983-June 1989 and June 30, 1989 is the
proposed Closing Date (Annex 7, Table 2). Historical disbursement profiles
for Indonesia are longer than projected for this Project, however because
final designs are in progress, procurement actions have begun and the
systems are relatively simple, an accelerated profile is appropriate.
4.09      In the event that the amounts to be disbursed total less than the
amount of the loan, the unused balance would be disbursed, as approved by
the Bank, against the purchase of goods and civil works; for the East Java
water supply program, additional consulting services, cr the continuation
of the training program.
F. Project Accounts and Audits
4.10      Project accounts would be maintained by the PMU and PIU showing,
for each contract, itemized expenditures based on measured units of work
certified by the supervising engineer. DSE has an established system of
project accounting which is satisfactory and it will introduce this to
the PMU. During negotiations assurances were obtained that project accounts
satisfactory to the Bank would be maintained, that project accounts and
statements of expenditures would be audited by GOI's Directorate General
for State Financial Control or others satisfactory to the Bank, and that the
audits would be made available to the Bank within seven months of the end of
the fiscal year.  The audit of PDAMs is discussed in para. 6.10.



- 23 -
V. POPULATION, SERVICE AND DEMLiD
A.  Present Water Service
5.01      At present eight of the proposed 50 beneficiary cities and towns
have public water systems, the balance depend variously on individual shallow
or deep wells, springs or river sources. Both the public and non-public sys-
tems are tenuous water sources because they are generally unreliable, both in
terms of quantity and quality. Most of the public systems are old, have leak-
age of 30% of more, and only serve from 2 to 8% of the population. All are in
need of expansion, and most, because of poor upkeep, require extensive repair
or replacement of equipment. Although house connections are generally
metered, about half of the meters are out of service.
B. Population Served and Service Levels
5.02      Twelve BNA cities and 37 IKK towns located within eight Kabupatens
and one Kotamadya are the beneficiaries of the Project. The assumptions made
in estimating water demand for the individual PDAM's are listed in Annex 8 and
are summarized in Table 5.1 below:

- 24 -
5.03      The Project will largely serve residential customers who will
recive 89% of total connections and 80% of the total consumption.  Half of
the beneficiaries would be urban poor, and about 46% of the total water
consumption will go to families with low and moderate income with water
consumption at 30 lpcd to 80 lpcd. About 60% of the residential house
connections are assumed to use less than 15 cu m per month, and none are
assumed to use more than 30 cu m per month. The original project objectives
set in 1980 were to achieve the 60% target of population served in 1986.
Under the revised construction schedule, and because of a downward estimate
for the rate of connections, this objective would be achieved by 1988. The
estimated demands are outgrowths of the planning and design criteria for the
BNA and IKK programs (para. 3.07 and Annex 4), but there is some risk that
the pace of house connections may be slower than planned, while the
consumption for some connections may be higher (para.  7.08).
C. Project Area and Service Area
5.04      it has not proved possible to lay down a single, simple definition
of the project area, since there is generally no town boundary which can be
used; if kecamatan boundaries were used, the project areas would generally
be too large and would cover low density rural areas. Hience, based on
engineering judgement, settlements within a reasonable reach of the town
(i.e., those to which the distribution system could eventually be extended,
say by year 1990) have been included as part of the project area.
Topographical features, population densitites, the views of Government
officials and future development plans have all been considered where these
are relevant to the project.
5.05      The 60% of people in the project area who will actually be supplied
generally comprise those where the service can be provided in the most
cost-effective manner. This is normally achieved by allocating supplies to
the town centre and the most densely populated parts of the town, and then
working outwards until the 60% figure is reached. This general policy has
been modified to take into account existing water supplies and sanitation
arrangements; for example, areas where the existing health hazards of
waterborne disease appear to be above average have been given priority.
Modifications have also been made where there is an unwillingness expressed
by the local people and desa officials to receive a piped supply.
5.06      On the above basis, a  service area' is defined to include all
the people who will receive the piped supply either by individual connections
or by standpipe. In certain situations (such as along a transmission main
supplying a town) households which are not in the project area can be
supplied at very low cost and source and transmissions works have been sized
accordingly. Similar provisions have been made for communities whose
existing water supplies are endangered by proposed spriqn  or wellfield
developments for the Project; in general standpipe installations are
provided for these communities.



- 25 -
D. Population and Demand Projections
5.07          Population  projections  were made  by  the  consultants  using  census
data. For each subproject the area to be served encompasses about 60% of
the area's total population (in accordance with the BNA planning criterion)
and is selected so facilities are provided in the most cost-effective
manner, usually the more densely populated town centers. The demand assumed
for various types of connections is shown in Table 3.1. It is likely that
unconstrained demand would exceed these allocations, but under the design
criteria BNA house connections would be metered and the progressive tariff
structure is expected to inhibit excess consumption. For IKK systems demand
will be limited by flow restrictors inserted in the service pipeline. The
demand summary for the eight PDAMs is given in Table 5.1., a typical demaind
analysis is in Annex 9, and the consolidated demand for each PDAM showing
the population served by house connectiorts and standpipes is in the financial
projections (Annex 10) and the monitoring indicator tables (Annex 12).
5.08          Under  the  Project  the  population with water  will  increase  from
7,100 to  796,000,  the  percentage  of  population  served  will  go  from  4.8%o
to 47.7%, the number of connections will increase 6 times and the volume
produced 4 times (Table 5.2). Compared to the volume of water per connec-
tion or per capita presently provided by the existing systems, after project
completion the unit volume will be reduced, in keeping with the objective to
provide smaller quantities of water to each person, but to extend service to
far more people.

VI.  FINANICIAL ANALYSIS
A. Existing Water Enterprises
6.01      Until recently water supply in Indonesia was operated by each
city, either as a department or as a PDAHI. But now that hundreds of new and
smaller systems are being built, their operations will be consolidated under
PDAMs which will be responsible for all systems within a kabupaten. This
is expected to provide economies of scale, better use of scarce human
resources, enable cross subsidization from the larger and wealthier cities
to the surrounding smaller towns, and reduce the level of tariffs required
to cover operating expenses. Malang, Madiun, Mojokerto, Sampang and
Sidoarjo have established PDAMs./l Situbondo has a temporary water enter-
prise operated by DSE, but this is scheduled for conversion to a PDAM.
Tuban and Tulungagung do not have water supply systems but PDAMs would be
established before project completion. Assurances were obtained during
negotiations that PDAMs would be established and staffed in the kabupatens
of Tuban and Tulungagung, and the temporary enterprise in Situbondo converted
to a PDAM, not later than December 31, 1984. Assurances were also be
obtained that assets provided under the Project will be inventoried and
transferred to the respective PDAMs shortly after construction is completed.
B.  Past Finances
6.02      There are no financial statements available for the periods when
the eight existing systems were operated as departments of their respective
kabupaten, and other than in Madiun the financial statements; for 1981 are
also not available. Statements were post-ante constructed by the consult-
ants and the chief accountant of each PDAM and are used as a starting point
for projections of future performance. Although the reliability of this
information is limited, it is not expected to affect the overall projections
since the future systems will be either totally new or many times larger
than the present systems.
6.03      The systems under operation in 1981 (with some exceptions for
Madiun, which has a better performance) share the following characteristics:
(a) accounting is on a cash rather than an accrual basis;
(b) financial information is limited and produced with long delay;
(c) the record of fixed assets is very limited;
(d)  depreciation is calculated but not incorporated in the accounting
records;
/1 Malang in June 1981, Madiun in March 1978, Mojokerto in January 1982,
Sampang in May 1980, Sidoarjo in July 1982.



- 27 
(e) billing and collection is satisfactory with receivables at about
10% of billing, mostly resulting from six-month delays in payments
by government offices;
(f) operations are subsidized by government in Madiun, Sampang and
Situbondo (payment of some salaries) but some cash surpluses are
produced in Malang and Mojokerto;
(g) labor productivity is low, 20 to 33 employees serve 1,000
connections, whereas a target of 10 is recommended by the
consultants for the overall PDAM (individual systems, especially
the small IKKs, may have a higher ratio, see Annex 11, Table 1);
(h) most connections are metered but many meters do not work;
(i) high charges and stringent payments terms for the installation
of house connections discourage connection applications;
(k) no guidance or assistance is provided in technical, financial
or managerial aspects; and
(k) although average tariffs are low in absolute terms (US$0.11
per cu m) they are not cheap considering the low level of service
provided.
C. Tariff Structure
6.04      In the mid 1970s DSE issued a Guideline for Tariff Structure which
prescribed unit price ratios for various consumer groups and levels of conr-
sumption. The guidelines are being revised jointly by Cipta Karya and
Ministry of Home Affairs to reduce the monthly consumption base from 15 to
10 cu m, and increase the progressivity for consumption above the base.
Standpipe consumers will pay 80% of the minimum unit tariff for house connec-
tions and IKK house connections will have flow limiting devices and be
charged at a fixed rate.  The ratios prescribed in the guidelines are not
mandatory and individual PDAMs are free to adjust them as long as they take
into account their obligation to meet the revenue covenant (para. 6.05).
GOI discussed with the Bank the proposed revised guidelines and agreed that
they would be further reviewed as a part of the tariff study.  The recommen-
dations of this study will be incorporated as appropriate in the national
guideline for tariff structure by December 31, 1984.
D. Revenue Covenant /1
6.05      Present service in the eight water systems under operation is
limited to a few thousand persons, with water rationed most of the day.
The existing PDAMs are financially weELk and their staff are inefficient and
untrained. Given the time required to bring about institutional improvement
/1 See footnote page 11.



- 28 -
and training, and the relative large size of new works compared to existing
facilities, normal financial covenants would not be meaningful before the
new works enter into operation. Agreement was reached during negotiations
that the minimum tariff for residential connections will be increased to
Rp 50 per cu m starting in mid-1983, and that consumption aLbove this level,
and the consumption of other users will be charged the progressive rates
established in DSE's Tariff Guideline. According to current Government
policy, PDAM's providing basic needs services must generate sufficient
revenues to at least cover operation, maintenance and depreciation costs.
Agreement was reached during negotiations that tariff and other revenues in
the Project PDAMs will be at least sufficient to cover operation, mainte-
nance and depreciation on revalued fixed assets staring in 1985. Since the
PDAMs have no previous debt service, and the Government and the Bank are
financing 100% of the capital expenditures, this would ensure that each PDAM
is financially viable, and the depreciation allowance will generate cash for
working capital or minor expansions. Agreement was also reached that the
depreciation allowance will be used exclusively for improvements or expan-
sions of the water systems or to pay debt service if recommended under the
tariff study. The average and minimum tariffs required to comply with the
revenue covenant are shown in Table 6.1. The average tariff (in 1983
prices) required in 1988 to meet this requirement would be Rp 94 per cu m
(US$0.13 per cu m).

mionth would average Rp 59 per cu m (Rp 42 to .'0 per cu -m) or about US$ 0.85

umonthly expenditures for stardpipe serv ice (Rp 224           4 cu -a per month)

affordable, i.e., .3-7% for average families and about 2% for low-income

6.08       Although initially onLy about 30% of the population is expected
tLo be provided with house connections, the government wants to promote
greater use of house connections.  It is therefore imporLant to 'fimit the
charge for nouse connections to its actual cost, and to provi de payment
facilities for connections. Assurances have been obtained that the charge
for the installation of domestic service connections would not exceed the
actual average cost and consumers would be permitted to pay for co-nnections
over three to five years.

6.09      Future Finances.  The projections (Annex 10) fo  the beneficiary
PDAMs consolidate the finances of the 50 subprojects plus some small systems
presently under operation.  They were prepared by Bank staff under assump-
tions given in Annex 11, and are based on selectively modified feasibility
report data (Document B.1 of Project File). The PDAMs may require start-up
subsidies in their early years of operation (as their systemz gear up to
full capacity) and allowances for this have been included in project costs.
Under the proposed revenue covenant (para. 6.05) the financial position of-
all PDAMs will be satisfactory when the new assets enter into operation in
1986.  Some selected financial data are shown in Table 6.2.
E. Reporting Requirements
6.10      In order to permit the satisfactory monitoring of project progress
by DSE, CK, Ministry of Home Affairs and the East Java Governor, assurances
were obtained during negotiations that:
(a)  starting with the period July-December L983, ses.i-annual reports
would be prepared by the Project Management Unit (PAU) covering:
(i) the status of project cost and implementation, land
acquisition and contracts, and (ii) statistical data for each
PDAM on the number of connections, volume sold and produced,
tariff and other revenues, operating expenses, number of
employees, average tariffs, number of days of accounts receivable,
and working ratio./l Copies of the reports should be sent to the
Bank within two months of the end of the reporting period;
(b)  Under the direction of the PMU, the PDAMs will establish and
maintain an accrual accounting system, these accounts will be
accessible to the Bank, will be audited annually commencing for
FY 1984 by GOI's Directorate General for State Financiaal Control
or others satisfactory to the Bank, and the Audit Report will be
sent to DSE, CX, Ministry of Home Affairs and the Bank not later
than June 30 of the following year; and
(c) CK will prepare a Project Completion Report, satisfactory to ttie
Bank, not later than 6 months after the closing date.

VII. ECONOMIC AND SOCIAL ANALYSIS
A. Justification
7.01      The population density of Java is higher, the poverty is greater,
and the water availability is less than elsewhere in Indonesia. External
assistance for Java's water supply development is allocated by agreement
with Government to the Dutch, the ADB and the Bank, who operate in West,
Central and East Java Provinces respectively. The proposed Project will
meet the basic water supply needs of the population in priority cities and
towns. The Project's systems are designed for low cost and rapid implement-
ation, and reaeh the poor at affordable prices.
7.02      Raising the health and living standards of the population is the
main purpose of the Project, but emphasis is also placed in the achievement
of sound institutional and financial policies for the sector, the strength-
ening of DSE and other sector institutions and the establishment of
financially viable and effectively managed PDAMs. The individual sub-
projects represent the least cost method for providing basic water service.
7.03      Marginal Cost.  The long-term marginal cost for water was esti-
mated using long-run Average Incremental Cost (AIC) at a LO% discount rate.
The AIC for the eight PDAM's is estimated (in 1983 prices) at Rp 191 per cu
m compared with the covenanted average tariff (in 1983 pr:ices) at Rp 87 per
cu m. The large disparity in the average tariff and the AIC is because a
large proportion (46%) of the water sales are to low-income consumers for
whom low tariffs are maintained as a matter of policy. Government expects
that low tariffs would promote the use of safe water to people who would
otherwise rely on unsafe alternative sources. However, under the tariff
structure guidelines (para. 6.04) charges for commercial, industrial and
residential users consuming more than 30 cu m per month would exceed AIC.
Higher tariffs than those proposed are not considered feasible until
consumer confidence in the new system is established.

7.04      Rates of Return.  Rates of return were calculated for each PDAM by
aggregating subprojects within its boundaries. Aggregation is appropriate
because the tariffs are uniform within each PDAM and there are no signifi-
canLt difLerences in the per capita cost of individual subprojects. Economic
rates of return (ERR) were calculated using the water charges paid at
present by consumers for purchases from water vendors for up to 15 lpcd, and
using average water charges for higher consumption as a surrogate of benefits.
ERR's so calculated average 4% and range from 3-6%. A 20% increase in
capital costs with an unchanged benefit stream would reduce the average ERR
to 3%, and with a 5% reduction in the benefit stream, would yield an average
ERR of 2% (Annex 16).  These calculations are not, however, a fair measure
of the economic rate of return because it does not adequately capture exter-
nalities (such as health benefits) which accrue to the community at large or
the benefits which result from the new systems for the present users in the
form of improved service. The low rates of return indicate economic
justification for higher tariffs, but this is not adopted at present because
the Government wants to promote community acceptance of safe, publicly
supplied water. The justification for the project does not rest with the
rate of return, but rather that it at least cost provides essential and safe
water supply service to a here-to-fore unserved population (more than half
of whom are poor), will help to improve the health and living standards of
the population and provides the means for strengthening sector institutions
and policies (paras. 7.01-7.03).



- 34 -
7.05      Population Benefited and Urban Poverty Impact.  The Project will
benefit about 720,000 people by 1988 through house connections and standpipes
(Table 7.2). The level of population served in the PDAMs will be increased
ten times, while present users will have improved water quality, service
pressure and reliability. Ninety-one percent of the total connections and 80%
of the distributed water will be for households, with government, commercial,
social and industrial users receiving the balance.
