7.07      The expected level of average water charges and the minimum charges
for house connections and standpipes is shown in Table 6.1. Under the
proposed tariff level and structure water service would be affordable to all
of the population, although only 60% would actually receive water services
at this time (para. 6.07).
B. Project Risks
7.08      Project risks are as follows:
(a)  The Provincial Cipta Karya and kabupaten staff have never
carried out water supply projects of the scope covered by this
Project, and how well they will perform is unknown. The risk
is mitigated by the establishmeni: of a Project Management Unit
which will draw upon more experienced staff and consulting
advisors, and the support to be given the PMU by Cipta Karya and
DSE.
(b)  Some design features are innovative and have not been extensively
tried in Indonesia. The purpose is to reduce costs, speed project
implementation and make service available to the poor. But it is
not known if there will be functional problems or reluctance by
consumers to accept this technology. The systems have been used
in the Philipines, and are now being installed in West Java where
they will be tested. During project execution consultants would
evaluate the design criteria and field performance of this
technology and make recommendations on its improvement (para.
3.07). If necessary and feasible, the designs will be modified
under the Project, and further improvements will be made in future
projects. The low cost of using these designs and the reasonable
certainty that they will work justifies the risk.
(c)  Most of the prospective consumers presently obtain their water
from rivers and shallow wells. It is sometimes difficult to wean
people away from these traditional sources, and there is some risk
that the Project will be underutilized. This risk is reduced
because during project preparation, the community leaders were
consulted by the project preparation teams to determine if they
wanted a new system (and were willing to pay the water charges),
and because the systems are nominally designed to serve only 60%
of the population. On the other side there is the risk that the
Project will be too successful, that is, that demand will rapidly
exceed available supply because the systems are designed to serve
only 60% of the population with only 60 lpcd. This would result
in short supply and the early need for further investments.  Under
the proposed revenue covenant, however, some excess cash would be
generated and this could be applied toward system improvement.



- 36 -
(d) Finally there is the substantial risk that the systems will not be
effectively and efficiently operated and maintained. Although
operation of the facilities is relatively simple, the PDAMs are
new and inexperienced in operations, staff are new and must be
trained, and sound public utility practices have to be introduced
and accepted. The training and institutional development of the
PDAMs, and the mechanisms to be established to provide assistance
and control of the PDA1s, mitigate this risk.
C. Environmental Impact
7.09      Because it provides safe, potable water the Project will have a
positive environmental impact and improve, the quality of life of persons in
the beneficiary cities and towns. During construction there will be some
disruption of vehicular and pedestrian traffic, and earth excavation will
increase sediment loads of storm water runoff, but these will be temporary
situations without major detrimental effects. After project completion the
volume of wastewater will increase, but this will be limited by virtue of
the low capacity of the water supply system; the expect volumes will be
readily handled by existing storm water canals and drains.  Moreover some
wastewater will probably be used for watering gardens and controlling dust.
The quality of the wastewater will not be obnoxious: it would at worst be
wash and laundry water and free of fecal matter. Water-borne fecal systems
do not exist, nor are they expected to come into being because of cost and
the limited flushing water volume available to households. Pourflush
latrines and pit privies are the commonly adopted methods for fecal waste
handling, and these are supported by government KIP and INPRES programs (see
para. 3.06).
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
8.01      During negotiations, assurances were obtained from GOI that:
(a) each PDAM will receive organization, management and accounting
assistance and will participate in training programs (para. 1-.24);
(b) DSE would arrange for a review of BNA and IKK design criteria
(para. 3.07);
(c) project accounts would be maintained and audited, and the audits
would be made available to the Bank (para. 4.10);
(d) service connections would be provided with meters or flow
restrictors, and the charge for domestic connections would not
exceed their average cost and be payable over three to five years
(para. 6.08).



- 37 -
(e) PDAMs would be established in Tuban, Tulungagung and Situbondo by
December 31, 1984 (para. 6.01);
(f) assets provided under the Project would be inventoried and trans-
ferred to the PDMIs within six months after commissioning
(para. 6.01);
(g) tariff guidelines would be reviewed and revised as appropriate
by December 31, 1984 (para. 6.04);
(h)  operational revenues of the beneficiary PDAMs will cover opera-
tion, maintenance and depreciation by 1985 and the depreciation
allowance would be used exclusively for water systems (para.
6.05); and
(i) a study would be undertaken of tariffs and mechanisms for cost
recovery and the agreed recommendations would be implemented in
Repelita IV (para. 6.06).
8.02       Subject to the above, the Project is suitable for a Bank loan of
US$30.6 million equivalent, for a term of 20 years including a grace period
of five years.

INDONESIA
FIRST EAST JAVA WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Training
Current Arrangements
A.        Training support for the urban water supply sector is provided by
the Sub-Directorate for Development (SDD) of DSE. SDD's training resources
comprise four main sections covering, respectively, management development,
technical development, laboratory, and training implementation. The
training implementation section includes a reasonably well equipped
residential training center for the provision of classroom instruction.
Except for a training laboratory, which is little used, there are no
facilities for the provision of practizal skills training.
2.        SDD's training repertoire comprises about ten standard course
programs broadly covering the subject areas of general, financial and
technical management of PDAMs, the supervision of water treatment and
distribution operations, and construction supervision. Most of these
courses are assembled into an annual training program geared to meet, to
the extent possible, the priority training needs of existing PDAMs and
those in process of being developed to PDAM status (BPAMs). Limited
training support is also provided to st'aff operating water systems being
developed under the IKK program. Standard training courses have been
offered at SDD's central training faci'Lity throughout the 1970s. Since
1979, the program has been increasingly decentralised to the provinces
through arrangements made with the development staff of DSE's provincial
offices (PABs). SDD's main training role, is therefore, to plan and
administer this annual program, and provide "software" support which
currently comprises an incomplete set of training manuals. The actual
delivery of the standard training programs is contracted to one of a panel
of local consultants most of whom are sanitary engineers but none appears
to have received substantial training .In the skills of training.
3.        The annual training program for FY1982/83 is given at Appendix I
to this Annex. Within the budgetary and staffing limitations of SDD, and
the extent to which PAB development staff can assist, the training program
for FY1983/84 is targetted to train a naximum of 2000 PDAM staff.  This
will represent about a threefold increase over the numbers trained during
FY 1982/83 but, without commensurate st:rengthening of SDD's training
resources for FY 1983/84, is unlikely t:o be realised.
Sector Manpower and Training Forecasts
4.        SDD has estimated that, currently, there are approximately 300
urban water supply systems in operation employing some 14,000 staff. In
the longer term (to 1990) GOI service targets for the water supply and



- 44 -                         ANNEX 2
Page 2
sanitation decade (as given in Section I of this report) would indicate
that the range of possible staffing requirements could be between 26,000 to
62,000. For indicative purposes a median figure of 44,000 staff is assumed
to represent the total staffing complement required for both BNA and IKK
programs at 1990. Allowing for existing staff and assuming linear growth
during the period to 1990, an annual sector recruitment rate of some 3,750
is indicated. The staff attrition rate for the urban water sector is
currently below 1.0% and is not expected to increase markedly over the
period. No allowance has therefore been made for this factor.
Project Manpower and Training Forecasts
5.        Using similar guidelines to those employed by SDD to develop
sector manpower forecasts, for the East Java Project DSE estimates that the
total staff required for beneficiary cities and towns will be 726 by 1987.
This represents an increase of about 100% over existing staffing levels.
SDD has prepared quite detailed manpower projections for East Java Province
as a whole, and is in the process of refining these data using the new MDP
Manpower Classification System (MCS). The SDD manpower projections for the
Province indicate an annual average training load of about 400 PDAM staff
(including IKK systems) over the period 1983-1990. Thie inference is that
if the proposed East Java Project is to address the toltal provincial urban
water sector manpower and training need, as opposed to project related
needs, an additional 1650 staff would be trained under the Project by 1987.
Training Strategy
6.        Recognising the inadequacy of current urban water supply training
support to meet this order-of-magnitude training load iFor new PDAM staff,
SDD has, since 1980, been attempting to accelerate the decentralization of
training services. The strategy is to build up local training capability,
province by province, with the focal point for training being, initially,
the development staff of PABs, but shifting in the longer term to become
the responsibility of provincial Cipta Karya. To assist the implementation
of this strategy, a Manpower Development Program (MDP) was initiated in
1980, with technical assistance provided by the Netherlands and
administered by the International Reference Center (IRC) for Community
Water Supply. The objective of the MDP/IRC was to lay the foundations for
the gradual development of a national training system for the urban water
sector as a whole. Initial concentration was to be upon 12 selected
provinces which would include those receiving bilateral and multilateral
donor assistance. Developmental activities of the MDPJ'IRC were targetted
to train regional (PAB) development assistants as trainers for the
provinces, devise a model MCS, prepare centrally produced "modular"1/
training materials, and provide training support and advisory services to
embryo provincial training units (PTUs).
1/   Training Module:  Training based upon building up knowledge and
skill in task-related units, as needed by an individual. A
training module could comprise both off and on-the-job training
elements and, possibly, an education element.



- 45 -                            ANNEX 2
Page 3
7.        Due to management and staffing problems, the MDP/IRC has achieved
only partial success and there is no apparent plan for the Netherlands to
continue support for this program beyond mid-1983. In February 1982 the
MDP/IRC was modified to be used primarily to re-inforce the implementation
of standard training courses offered annually by the SDD Training Section.
Responsibility for the production of MDP training materials was transferred
to a production team comprising of consortia of Dutch consulting firms
(DHV, TGI and IWACO) who will complete a first batch of 25 training manuals
(comprising 217 modules, or one-half of the total number required), by
mid-January 1983. Upon completion of this task, and the completion of the
Netherlands assisted Eleven Cities training project in July 1983, other
than training support for IKK schemes, Netherlands funding for urban water
sector manpower development may be halted.
Proposed East Java Project Training Component
8.        Cipta Karya's training strategy for the urban water sector is
consistent with GOI policy to promote regional autonomy through development
projects and, also, with the Bank's aim to assist institutional development
within the sector. Although a substantial sector-oriented training
component was included in the Bank-financed Second Water Supply Project
(Loan 1709), implementation of the plan was deferred, at the request of
GOI, in order to allow the Netherlands funded MDP to take precedence.
9.        Therefore the training component of the First East Java Water
Supply Project is designed to continue, and further develop, the
implementation of Cipta Karya's training strategy for the urban water
supply sector. It is also designed to be integrated with the overall MPW
training strategy to establish a coordinated manpower development program.
To achieve this, the scope of the training component extends beyond meeting
the manpower and training needs associated with the subprojects in 13
cities and 37 IKK towns. It would additionally:
(a)  Establish a permanent provincial training unit (PTU) to be
developed, initially, as a training arm of the East Java PAB.
Although the PTU would give priority to assisting with the
recruitment and training needs of the Project beneficiary cities
and towns, manpower development services would also be available
to other BNA and IKK projects in the East Java Province. PTU
training services would contribute to the planning and design of
urban water supply development as well as meeting manpower and
training needs generated by construction, management, operation
and maintenance of water installations.Subject to appropriate
local modifications, the proposed PTU would be modelled upon the
successful PTU established by the Dutch consulting firm DHV at
Medan to support the North Sumatra Eleven Cities Project. The
East Java PTU would be developed with the view to its eventual
upgrading as one of the six Regional Training Centers2/ for the
urban water sector.
2/   In the longer term, (1985-90), Cipta Karya would consider
establishing regional training centers to support urban (and
possibly rural) water supply training, based at: North Sumatra
(Medan), South Sumatra (Palembang), West Java (Bandung), Mid-Java
(Semarang), East Java (Surabaya) and South Sulawesi (Ujung
Pandang).



- 46 -                        ANNEX 2
Page 4
(b) Strengthen SDD's ability to fulfill its role as the central
training resource for the urban water supply sector through the
provision of training services which would include, as a minimum:
(i)  the central provision of training courses, workshops and
seminars for urban and municipal PDAM managers and senior
staff;
(ii) implementation of the planned "crash" training program
through expansion of the existing SDD field training
program, to provide both and off and on-the-job training,
for the key staff of PDAMs and project management units
(PMU) (particularly construction supervisors) with priority
allocation to BNA and IKK programs in provinces not
supported by PTUs.
(iii) the development and commissioning of the currently
under-utilized central laboratory training facility;
(iv)  furtner development of the MDP to include the provision of
training advisory and support services to PTUs and, where
PTUs have not yet been established, to existing
regional/provincial training development staff;
(v)  installation of a manpower planning and forecasting service
incorporating, and refining in the light of experience, the
existing Manpower Classification System (MCS);
(vi) the provision a standard range of "training of trainers"
programs, covering appreciation seminars for PDAM/BPAM
managers, full-time PTU staff and instructors, regional/PAB
development assistants, BNA/IKK project management staff,
and local consultants retained to assist with the
implementation of SDD training courses;
(vii) further development of the MCS to include a battery of
experimental skill/aptitude tests and selection criteria to
assist with the recruitment of PDAM staff; and
(viii) the establishment of a central training media development
function to continue the work of the MIDP production team
currently comprising a consortia of Dutchconsultants; and
(c) Provide the means to extend Cipta Karya's training strategy
elsewhere in the urban water sector by developing, concurrent
with the East Java PTU, a "pre-construction" training program in
one, or more, provinces to be selected by DSE (for example, South
Sulawesi). This would lead to, and assist, the planned and
timely establishment of additional PTUs capable of meeting
training needs generated by the construction, operation and
maintenance phases of urban water supply development in the
selected provinces.



- 47 -                      ANNEX 2
Page 5
10.       Achievement of these broader training objectives would make
substantial progress towards the implementation of Cipta Karya's training
strategy for the urban water sector. Regional/provincial training
resources would, by 1987, be permanently established in East Java and South
Sulawesi and North Sumatra/Aceh and West Java. Moreover, the central role
and resources of SDD would be strengthened to facilitate the development of
additional PTUs at Palembang (serving South Sumatra) and Semarang (serving
Central Java). The next Bank-financed project for urban water sector
development could assist in the completion of this aspect of the sector
training plan so that, by about 1990, the first six regional/provincial
training centers would be established.
Training Program Content and Related Data
11.       Data is available in the project files covering:
(a) Training program curricula and job descriptions for seven key
PDAM staff positions;
(b) Training curricula and job descriptions for Regional/Provincial
Manpower Development Assistants;
(c) Guidelines for staffing and training project management unit
personnel;
(d) Outline specification and staffing profile for a typical PTU; and
(e) Subject areas to be covered by 60 training manuals currently
being prepared by the MDP Production team, together with the
breakdown of manuals into constituent "task-specific" training
modules.
Cost Estimate
12e       The total cost of the project training component, including
approximately 110 man-months of technical assistance, is estimated to be
US$2.0 million with a foreign exchange component of US$1.5 million. This
budget estimate is based upon the following assumptions:
(a) the cost of preparing the 35 additional training manuals required
to complete the full set of 60 manuals identified by the MDP
production team, would have been met by uncommitted training
funds in Loan 1709-IN; and
(b) the Netherlands technical assistance for training support to the
urban water sector would focus more upon the IKK Program in
provinces not covered by the proposed East Java Water Supply
Project training component.
The cost breakdown of the proposed training component is given at Appendix
2 of this Annex.

Summary of Technical Assistance and Studies
A. Project Implementation Services
i.    Project Management Advisory Services.  To provide project manage-
ment expertise to the Project Management and Project Imp'lementation Units.
This would include services in the following activities: liaison with
government offices and consultants during preparation of final design and
during procurement, construction and start-up; procurement (advertising,
bidding, evaluating and awarding); contracting; reporting, accounting,
monitoring and controlling project activities; receiving and handling design
and contracttual queries and changes; providing specialized advisory services
(such as ground water drilling); preparing for startup of facilities;
and on-the-job training in project implementation and facility operation.
Estimated /1 consulting staff: Advisors (F), 72 mm;
Advisors (L), 108 mm /2

and local government. Provide specialized services such as inspections at
equipment manufacturing plants.  Assist in preparation of as-built drawings.
Estimated consulting staff: Engineers (F), 60 mm;
Engineers (L), 288 mnm; Technicians (L) 280 mm.
B.  Sector Policy Studies, Institutional Support and Training /3

management, accounting, administrative and personnel systems in PDAMs and
training staff in their use. Prepare microcomputer software package for
billing, payroll, stores, accounting and management systems.

4.        Tariffs, Financial Viability, Cost Recovery and Affordability.
Using the beneficiary PDAMs as case studies, analyze the required average
tariffs and tariff structure which would generate funds for operation,
maintenance, and different levels of capital pay-back, all while keeping
tariffs at levels which are affordable to the poor (by way of cross subsidy
and unit price tariffs which increase with volume used). Prepare revised
financial projections and show how cost recovery can reduce the Government s
financial burden or increase water supply development. Identify appropriate
mechanisms for recovery costs (for example, by financing investments with
loans under different terms) and appropriate recipients for the recovered
capital funds (i.e., central, provincial or kabupaten governments). To be
done in conjunction with Item 3, PDAM Institutional Development. No
specific man-month allocation.

INDONESIA
EAST JAVA WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Planning and Design Criteria
I. Planning Criteria
A. Selection of Beneficiary Towns
The beneficiary towns were sel.ected by Cipta Karya in consultation
with the Ministry of Home Affairs and provincial officials. Factors
considered in selecting towns were: simplicity (favoring towns easily
supplied by ground or spring waters); population size (favoring the larger
towns); need (favoring places which have not received earlier support from
Government for water supply and/or where incidence of waterborne disease or
poverty is high); other Government programs (where water would contribute to
other development efforts); and the poltential for economic growth. If in the
course of the Project substitute towns are proposed, they would fulfill the
above criteria.
B. Existing Systems
Existing facilities would be integrated into proposed systems where
expected service life justifies and abandoned if expectations are for high
leakage, operating costs or repair frequency. Existing systems in the BNA
cities of M4adiun, Batu and Kapangen will be integrated. There are no existing
systems in IKK towns.
C. Project and Service Area
The physical objective is to provide an average domestic supply of
60 lpcd in BNA cities and 45 lpcd in IKK towns to about 60% of the 1985
population within project area. The areas to be served are those where
service can be extended in the most cost-effective manner, usually the more
densely populated town centers. Higher priority is given to areas which
have poor present water service, poor sanitation, or adverse health
experiences. Service is not provided where local people and officials have
expressed unwillingness to receive service. Standpipe service is provided
to persons near springs (particularly if the spring has been the traditional
source) and along transmission lines, even if such persons live outside the
project area. Hence subprojects generally serve more than 60% of the target
population.
D. Water Quality
WHO highest desirable level, but consideration given to Indonesian
standards; chlorine dosing capacity: 3 mg of free chlorine per liter;



- 5-                                   ANNEX 4
Page 2
average chlorine dosing 1.5 mg of free chlorine per liter; not less than two
chlorinators per system.
E. Water Service Levels
The water supply service levels and allocation of water for
domestic consumers is shown in the following table:
WATER SUPPLY SERVICE LEVEL AND ALLOCATION
z of
Average       service       X of total
consumption       area         domestic
Category                                      (lpcd)       population   consumption
BNA
House connection (irmtefed)                  120             25             50
Yard connection (metered)                      60             25             25
Standpipe (metered)                            30            50              25
All domestic consumers                         60            100 /a         100
IKK
House connection (flow restricted)             60             50             67
Standpipe (metered)                            30             50             33
All domestic consumers                         45            100 /a         100
/a  Represents about 60% of the community's total population.
Under BNA standards, the water allocation for institutions will be per DSE
standard allowances by category of institution or 25% of domestic demand,
whichever is lower. Losses are allowed for at least 15%' of total consumption
maximum day flow is 1.15 times average day, lumpy investments (such as
transmission mains) are sized at 1.43 times the 1985 maximum day demand, and
there is no allocation for fire flow. Under IKK standards non-domestic
consumption is allocated at 5% of the domestic allocation, losses are 15% of
total consumption, maximum day is 1.1 times average day, and there is no
maximum hour or fire flow allocation.
II. BNA Design Criteria and Checklist
A. Standard Designs and Specifications
Standard DSE designs-are used for: elevated storage tanks of 300,
500, 750 and 1,000 cu m; generator installation; break pressure tanks;
pipeline details; standpipe and house connection details; general conditions
for civil works and for local and international procurement of goods; and
technical specifications for pipes and appurtenances.



- 55 -                            ANNEX 4
Page 3
C. Wells
-  Not less than two per subproject;
-  Well diameter, casing and screen designed for maximum yield;
submersible pumps for maximum day demand.
-  Well to include valving and flow meter, which will be located
3 m from well head to allow future drill rig access.
-  Well casing and screen of mild or low carbon steel, screen
galvanized.
E. Storage Reservoirs
- Soil analysis will be undertaken to ensure structural
stability.
- Total capacity about 20% of maximum day demand.
F. Pipelines
-  Break pressure tank if pressure exceeds 10 bar.
-  Consumers close to pipelines served even if they are outside
project area, balancing storage volume of 60 cu m provided
for such service where economically feasible.
-  Gate valves located each 2 km.
-  Air valves at highpoints, flushing valves at low points.
-  Analysis and provision for surge on long lines.
-  Swabbing points at pipeline ends and at each gate valve.
-  Soil analysis (resistivity, pH, redox, sulphate and chloride)
to assess potential for external corrosion.
-   later analysis (Asbestos Cement Index, Langelier Index, Ryznar
Stability Index) to assess potential for internal corrosion.
-  Distribution pipe design criteria:  maximum hour = 1.0 maximum
day; maximum velocity 2 m/sec at maximum hour; minimum pressure
1.0 bar at maximum hour; maximum pressure 7.5 bar; valves on
200 m grid.
-  Fire hydrants:  on pipe 100 rm diameter and larger, each 300 m
and at pipe ends for flushing.



- 56 -                              ANNEX 4
Page 4
G. Service Connections
- House connection: 20 mm /1 service pipe and 15 mm meter.
- Yard connection: 15 mm service pipe and 15 mm meter.
- Standpipe: 40 mm service pipe and meter; four bibtaps; spaced
for maximum 200 m haul distance and maximum 200 persons per
standpipe.
H. Control Systems and Instrumentation /2
(i) For pumps, manual if not more than two pumps in close proximity;
instrumentation to indicate flow rate and quantity, and water level and
pressure. Audible alarms for overflows. For remote sources, control by
float operated valve in storage reservoir and pressure activated switch on
transmission line.
(ii) Pumps protected by low water level cutouts and relux valve micro
switches to indicate flow failure. Instantaneous flow rate and totalizer
flow meters to be located at well head and storage tank outlets.
(iii) At startup of facilities operating procedures will be developed to
optimize the time of pumping, to enable maximum use of off-peak power rates.
I. Buildings
Office buildings sized at 6 sq m per office worker will be provided
under the Project if space is not presently available. Each subproject will
be provided with a standard workshop of 84 sq m, and a major workshop will
be built for each PDAM headquarters. Generators and chlorinators will be
housed in buildings dedicated to such purposes. Guard houses will be
provided where security needs dictate.
III. IKK Design Criteria and Checklist
A. IKK Standard Systems
The IKK subprojects are a part of Government's effort to rapidly
build water supply systems in numerous kecamaten headquarter towns, to which
end DSE has prepared standardized modules to simplify design, procurement,
/_ Subject to review by DSE to determine if 15 or 20 mm is more cost
effective.
/2  Reference for more detail:  Main Report, East Java IBRD Water Supply
Project; Sir M. MacDonald and Partners, August 1982; Section 5.14 (Docu-
ment B.1 in Project File).



-57 -                              ANiNEX 4
Page 5
corLstruction and stockpiling. IKK service levels and allocations are given
in I.(E.) above. System capacities are standardized as shown in below:
Design            Source    No. of house                           Population
population         capacity   connections    No. of standpipes         served
lps
3,600 - 7,200       2.5          180                  9               3,600
7,200 - 14,400      5.0          360                 18               7,200
14,400 - 20,000    10.0           720                 36              14,400
D. Standard Designs and Specifications
DSE has standard designs for IKK wells, well heads, service
connectors and standpipes.  Standard civil works and equipment specifications
for IKK subprojects are the same as for BNA subprojects. The more unique
feature of the design is that, in order to reduce capital and operating
costs, one day's household supply is provided over 24 hours, i.e., there is
no ability to meet instantaneous and peak demands. This lowers pipe costs
andl eliminates meters, meter reading and volume-based billing.
E. Storage Reservoirs
No storage is provided in IKK systems. Hlowever, each household
provides a traditional in-house tank of about 1 cu m capacity.
F. Buildings
Office buildings and workshop space will not be provided.
IV. Review of BNA and IKK
A review would be conducted of present BNA and IKK design
criteria. The review would cover the theoretical aspects of design and also
the experiences of actual installations, with regard to practicality,
economy and consumer acceptance. The review would address questions such
as:
(a) Is the 60% target service level appropriate and economic?
(b) Can the high speed design methods developed by DSE be
improved to produce more efficient designs?



- 58 -                             ANNEX 4
Page 6
(c) Is the time horizon for design capacity adequate? What about for
lumpy investments?
(d)  Do consumers accept low flow systems which require in-house
storage? Should the systems be designed to accomodate
extensive conversions from low flow connections to metered
connections which could meet peak demands?
(e) What is an appropriate water allocation for commercial and
industrial connections in BNA and IKK systems?
(f) Should IKK systems have service storage? (At present there is
no provision for such storage).
(g)  Does the source selection procedure of DSE ensure that the
least cost water source is chosen?
(h) Are the mechanisms for testing if the community wants and needs
the proposed wate- system appropriate, and if not, how can they be
improved? How can there be more advanced consu:Ltation with
the communities?
(i) What is the "bad debt" experience from house connections and
standpipes?
(j) Is the lumpiness of IKK connection numbers (i.e., in lumps
of 180, 360 or 720 connections) appropriate and efficient?
Are limits on numbers of connections appropriate, or should the
numbers be determined by actual count of prospective consumers done
in consultation with local officials?
(k) To what extent should systems be designed with standby facilities
to enhance reliability?
(1) Consideration is being given to providing partial day service in
the initial years of operation (to reduce operating costs,
especially where electric generators are required for pumping).
What are the implications of this, considering: (i) the distri-
bution systems restricted connections are designed for average
flow rates, i.e., a uniform flow over a 24 hour period; (ii)
possible contamination of water in distribution system; (iii)
potential capital and operating cost saving; and (iv) possible
design changes, and changes in operating procedures.
(m)  Should IKK systems invariably have two wells each, (one as standby)
even if not required to meet water demand?



- 59 -MANNEX 4
Page 7
(in) Should IKK systems unvariably have two wells each (one as
standby), even if not required to meet water demand?
(n) House connections are designed for 10 persons per connection,
yet census data shows the on average only 5 persons live in a
household. CK explains that it uses 10 persons on the assumption
that two houses will draw water from each connection. What are
the design and cost implications of this practice? Should it be
changed?

- 63 -                             ANNEX 6
Page 1
INDONESIA
EAST JAVA WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Project Implementation and Supervision
A. Implementation Program
1.        Project implementation is divided into the following ten main areas
of activity which would be carried out as shown in Chart 5.
(a) Engineering services for the ENA City and IKK town subprojects,
'covering studies, surveys and design;
(b) Engineering services for project management and construction
supervision;
(c) Land acquisition;
(d) Bulk procurement of pipes, well screens and casings, and related
equipment;
(e) Bulk procurement of electrical and mechanical (E and M) equipment
and supplies;
(f) Civil works, covering the contracts for all general construction
and pipelaying;
(g) Well construction, covering completion of all deep wells;
(h) Other equipment such as drill rigs and training materials,
purchased separately and not under bulk procurement;
(i) Other technical assistance and studies; and
(j) Training.
2.        Engineering Design Services for the BNA and IKK Subprojects.  The
preliminary design studies for the 13 cities were completed by October 1982
and the final designs would be finished by May 1983. Final design and
cost estimates for the IKK subprojects of those towns which do not depend on
groundwater drilling are complete. Final design for the towns where
groundwater drilling is required will have to await completion of drilling,
scheduled for February 1983.
3.        Advisory Services for Project. Management and Construction
Supervision.  These services provide project management expertise to the



ANNEX 6
-- 64 -                             Page 2
Project Management and Project Implementation Units, covering procurement,
contracting, liason, critical path analysis, construction supervision and
start-up. The functions of the PMU and PIU are described in the Project
File, which also contains TOR for the advisory services. About four foreign
and numerous local engineers and technicians would be required. The work
would begin about May 1983 with two people, and the staff would gradually
be built up to full complement by 1984. In addition, several Indonesian
consulting firms would assist in the field supervision of construction and
in plant inspections of goods during manufacture as necessary. Consulting
firms to be employed have been designated.
4.         Other Technical Assistance and Studies.  The Project includes
technical assistance to help develop PDAM organizations and to continue the
support for DSE begun under earlier water supply and urban development
loans. Studies are in the area of finance, tariffs, institutional
arrangements, design criteria and the preparation of future projects.
Technical assistance to PDAMs and studies would be phased over the period
1984-87, starting with existing PDAMs and progressing to those formed in the
later stages of the Project. Annex 3 describes technical assistance and
studies in greater detail.
5.         Training.  Training is in the areas of PDAM management and
operations, and includes administrative and manual skills. Training will
take place over the period June 1984-December 1987. Annex 2 describes the
program.
6.         Land.  Land acquisition must also be completed quickly, to allow
the final site investigations to be done and to confirm land availability
for the final designs. Land requirements have been defined by the
consultants and land acquisition has been initiated by DSE, the East Java
government and the beneficiary kabupatens.
7.         Bulk Procurement.  The bulk procurement documents will allow for
the provision of the equipment quantities envisaged at preliminary design
stage with options to adjust the quantities by moderate amounts. Bulk
procurement contracts will cover:
(a)  Pipeline equipment and supplies;
(b)  Well screens and casings;
(c)  Water meters for production facilities;
(d)  Pumps, motors and controllers, including associated pipework,
valves;
(e)  Generator sets and electrical appurtenances; and
(f)  Chlorination equipment.



ANNEX 6
5-                                 Page 3
8.        Pipeline Equipment and Supplies.  Pipeline equipment and supplies
include procurement of all transmission and distribution pipework and
valves, all pipework and valves for service connections, flowmeters,
restrictors and similar items. This heading also covers all special tools
and equipment needed for installation and testing of the above items.
9.        The procurement of pipes is normally the critical path item
because a period of 11 months must be allowed for bidding, evaluation and
award, and another 7 months for the initial delivery of goods (4 months for
fabrication and 3 months for shipping and customs clearance). The draft
tender documents have been prepared and approved by the Bank and the call
for bids has been issued. In order to expedite the project CK will draw
on existing stocks of pipe and accessories (and well casings and screens,
see para. 1) until equivalent goods, procured under the project, are
delivered. Stocks so drawn will be then also be replaced. CK has assured
that goods in stock which will be used for the project fully meet the tech-
nical specifications approved by the Bank.
10.       Electrical-Mechanical Equipment and Supplies.  The timing for
these items is similar to that for the pipeline equipment and supplies:
award of contracts in January 1984. However most of the electrical and
mechanical equipment is not needed at such an early stage in the Projects
construction and delivery can be programed to match the requirements of the
other contracts. The first main requirement will be for the well pumps and
equipment, forwarding pumps, control equipment and other items, for the
first IKK subprojects towns in December 1984.
11.       Well Screens and Casings.  Twenty six IKK subprojects will be
supplied by wells. Of these 17 wells are already in place; they were
originally drilled under the exploration program and will be converted to
production wells. The same is true for eight of the 24 wells required for
the nine BNA towns which will be served by wells. As described in para. 9,
well casings and screens required prior to the delivery of project -
procured goods would be drawn from existing stocks and subsequently
replaced.
12.       Civil Works.  Preparation of tender documents is proceeding
simultaneously with the final design work. Indonesian standard documents
will be used. For the 13 BNA subprojects, the documents should be
completed by end of March 1983, while the IKK documents should be completed
two months later.
13.       The first batch of civil works contracts would be bid in the last
half of 1983. Bidding periods are scheduled to take two months, followed by
two month periods for the evaluation of bid, approval of the recommendations
and award of tender.



ANNEX 6
- 66 -                            Page 4
14.        Civil works contracts would be phased to spread out the work of
evaluating and awarding the large numbers of contracts involved. Con-
tractors for the first batch of towns (Batch A) would start construction
in 1984; Batches B and C follow in two month intervals. Provisional
allocation of the batches has been based on achieving a balance of BNA and
IKK subprojects. Batch A covers Madiun, Sampang and Tulungagung; Batch B,
Malang and Tuban; Batch C, Situbondo, Mohokerto and Sidoarjo.
15.        The allocation of service connections to individual consumers
should be decided by the PDAM staff not later than March 1984 so contractors
can make connections while laying mains. All connections under the Project
should be completed by the end of 1987.
16.       Well Construction.  The program for well construction (other than
that already done under the exploration program, see para. 11) follows a
similar pattern to that for the civil works: documents prepared by December
1983 and work on site starting in July 1984. This program would allow bulk
procured well-screen and casing to be used in the wells with delivery of
these supplies in April 1984.
17.        Other Equipment.  This covers equipment which is not purchased
under the bulk procurement procedures. The principal items are: vehicles,/l
office equipment and furniture, and possibly laboratory and workshop
equipment.
B. Contract Arrangements
18.        Contracts are divided into five types:
(a)  civil works construction for the BNA  -.&bprojects;
(b)  civil works construction for the IKK subprojects;
(c)  civil work construction for any PDAM Head Office in a town
where there is no new waterworks system;
(d)  groundwater development, to provide deep drilled wells;
(e)  procurement of equipment and supplies for all the subprojects.
19.        Civil Works Construction.  Civil works contracts are small and
widely scattered in area and time span. They would be carried out following
local competitive bidding procedures. They would be grouped (where feasible
/1 Because GOI does not allow importation of vehicles, all motor vehicles
and cycles would be procured locally following GOI procedures, and
Bank loan would not disburse against them.



ANNEX 6
_-, -                                Page 5
in packages which would exceed US$320,000 in value) to encourage the
participation of large local contractors or joint ventures of small local
firms. A tentative listing of contracts is given in Attachment 1 to this
Annex.
20.       For the BNA systems, two packages have been adopted:  one would
cover all general construction work for the source works, transmission main,
service reservoir, offices and other buildings; the other would cover all
the pipelaying for the distribution system, including installation of the
service connections. The general value of each contract package is expected
to lie in the range of Rp 100 million to 1,000 million, at December 1982
price levels.
21.       For the IKK subprojects, the value of the construction work in an
individual town will be quite small. Thus a single contract would be
sufficient for each town; in fact, it may be found that several IKK schemes
which are sited close together could be combined into a single contract.
On the assumption of an individual contract for each town, the average
value is likely to range between Rp 25 million to 100 million.
22.       The [lead Offices for four of the Kabupaten PDAMs will be located
in towns where water supply schemes will be under construction for the
Project. In these cases, construction of the Head Office should be
included in the general construction contract for the town.  lowever, for
the other three Kabupatens, the Head Office will be a town where no other
construction work is in progress for the Project. In these cases the use
of existing Government or other office space would be considered, but
separate contracts have been tentatively listed and included in the Project.
23.       The BNA subprojects will need about 16 additional deep wells (plus
conversion work for the eight existing exploratory holes); the work has been
split into two contracts. The number of wells to be drilled for the IKK
subprojects depends on the results of the ongoing investigations contract
and on the policy adopted for the number of wells per town. On the
assumption that 26 wells are required, a set of three drilling contracts is
proposed. Thus the value of the groundwater drilling contracts are expected
to lie in the range of Rp 80 million to 100 million; in all cases of work
will cover construction of the well alon,e, without pumping equipment and
pipework.
24.       Procurement of Equipment and Supplies.  Procurement of equipment
and supplies would be done in nine packages covering: pipes and fittings
(2 packages), borehole screen and casing, water meters, electrical/
mechanical equipment, chlorination equipment, vehicles, drill rigs and
miscellaneous items. 'lost of these items would be obtained under the normal
ICB procedures; some of the packages, such as for miscellaneous equipment,
may be covered by other procurement arrangements and vehicles would be
procured under GOI procedures. All procurement contracts will be adminis-
tered centrally, from the DSE and Cipta Karya offices in Jakarta.



ANNEX 6
- 68 -                             Page 6
25.       Bulk procured items would be delivered to the headquarters town of
each of the Kabupatens. This will avoid the need for a very large stores
compound in Surabaya and some double-handling and transport costs.
C. Project Management
26.       Project Implementation and Project Management Units would be
established comprising of two central units, located in Surabaya, and eight
subunits. Each subunit would supervise all construction work within one
kabupaten. The largest subunit (for Malang Kabupaten) would be responsible
for four BNA schemes and nine IKK schemes; the smallest number of schemes
under one subunit would be the single subproject for Kotamadya Madiun.
Specialist advisers would be located in the PIU and PMU. The functions of
the PIU and PMU are described in item B.2.4 of the Project File.
27. The Units to be established in Surabaya will be responsible for:
(a) overseeing work of consultants during preparation of final
designs and contract documents;
(b) liaison with all local government offices, at provincial,
kabupaten and kecamatan levels;
(c) liaison with the central offices in Jakarta of DSE, Cipta Karya
and other agencies;
(d)  approval of all contract documents for construction, issuing
of documents, appraisal of tenders and award of contracts;
(e) supervision of construction for all these contracts, through the
PIU subunits in the kabupatens;
(f) maintaining records and project accounts;
(g) receiving and dealing with design queries and changes initiated
by the subunits;
(h) coordinating the procedures for procurement, delivery and storage
of equipment and supplies (the actual contracts will be adminis-
tered centrally).
(i) assisting in start-up of facilities; and
(j)  assisting in training of construction supervisory staff and PDAM
operating personnel.
28.       The staff in each subunit would be responsible for administering
all the construction contracts within their kabupaten. Assisted by
consultants, they would undertake all the normal site supervision duties,
would check contractor's statements for payments, review contractual



ANNEX 6
Page 7
problems as they arise and initiate queries, improvements or changes
required for the detailed designs.
29.       The PMU, PIU and subunits would be staffed by integrated teams
drawn from DSE/Cipta Karya offices and from consultants.

ASSUMPTIONS USED IN THE FI]NANCIAL PROJECTIONS /1
Existing Financial Statements
1.        Other than Madium, the six PDAMs which have water supply systems
have precarious operations, low percentage of population served, water
rationing, and staff which have not the experience or training required for
public utilities. These deficiencies are a common factor for the project
PDAMs (see para. 6.02).  Accounting is rudimentary and the financial results
for 1981 were not available when the feasibility report was prepared.
Furthermore there is no accounting information previous to the establishment
of these PDAMs in 1981. Financial data for 1981 and estimates for 1982 were
then prepared by the consultants during the feasibility studies. The
financial information information available is therefore limited and not
fully reliable. Financial projections for the period 1981-90 were prepared
by consultants as a part of the feasibiliLty study, and revised by Bank staff.
The deficiencies of the feasibility reports and financial data are noted
in the Project File and have been discussed with CK. These projections will
be revised in detail during the preparation of the tariff studies.
Revised financial projections
2.        Financial projections are shown in Annex 10.  Consultants esti-
mates for 1982-83 were maintained where appropriate.  The main modifica-
tions made by Bank staff and the financial assumptions are described below.
3.        Demand.  In the feasibility reports practically all connections
and population benefitted is assumed served by 1986. Given the project
construction schedule, some of the IKKS would be completed in early 1985.
Therefore the revised projections include some connections and volume sold
in this year. On the other hand, it is unlikely that the forecasted large
increase in connection and population served would be achieved by 1987,
especially since in some IKKs the capacity being provided would serve more
than 90% of the population, and its is difficult to reach these levels of
service during project construction. In the appaisal projections the
population and connections targets are reached only by 1988, although it
takes up to 1990 for IKKs where the capacity has been provided to serve up
to 90% or more of the forecasted population.
4.        Population.  No separate analysis of demand by towns or IKKs was
presented in the report. Detail anlaysis of population and levels of
services was made during the appraisal for the cities and IKKs included in
the project and those others, served or not, under the PDAM-s jurisdiction
(see Annex 9, typical Demand Analysis). The reasons above explain why the
annual targets for population, connection, volume sold etc. are different
between the appraisal and feasibility reports. The overall capacity is the
same. Population forecast are based in the 1980 census and actual surveys
by consultants. The population with water include all users and the popula-
tion served refers to house connection service.
/1 See footnote page 11.



- 1o4 -                          AN;NEX 11
Page 2
5.        Water production.  Unaccounted-for-water was reported at very low
levels (below 3%) in some PDAMs. Since this is not feasible the volume sold
was adjusted to reflect at least 15-20% unaccounted-for-water. After the
project completion, the design target of 15% unaccounted-for-water was
maintained (but increased for PDAMs were the old system is a significant
part), since the water systems are going to be practically new. In Madium a
leak detection study combined with the new systems built, is expected to
reduce the unaccounted-for-water to 17% by 1983. These assumptions are
optimistic but consistent with the design parameters.
6.        Financing.  The feasibility report assumed that half of the project
works would be financed by soft-terms loan. Financial projections prepared
during the appraisal (available in Project File), show that debt financing
is affordable if soft term conditions are provided. The government s present
policies of grant financing have been accepted by the Bank, pending the
result of financial and tariff studies convenanted under the Project (para.
6.06). The appraisal projections are based on grant financing and levels of
tariffs and other revenues (house connections) which would cover all operat-
ing expenses and the revalued depreciation of fixed assets in operation i.e.
0% rate of return, starting in 1985.
7.        Other operating revenues.  Other operating revenues include
meter-maintenance charges, charges for late payment etc. However, the main
source are connection charges. Since the respective installation cost is
financed with the other capital expenditures, and the connection belong to
the PDAM, these charges are considered an alternative to tariffs which would
recover the capital expenditures. This also allows all revenues to be
controlled by the revenue covenant. The feasibility report assumes that the
average charges for residential consumers and industrial connections will be
about Rp 70,000 and Rp 90,000 respectively (in 1982 prices). That report
also assumes that residential consumers will be allowed to pay these charges
in installments during a five year period (free of interest), and that others
users will be allowed to pay during two years. These assumptions have been
maintained, but the charges for house connections were reduced to Rp 40,000,
which is considered the average cost of connections in cities and IKKs, and
would be affordable to the population. Assurances were obtained that resi-
dential consumers will be allowed to pay the house connection cost over
three to five years. The annual values used for the financial statements
(Annex 10), represent the expected annual collection from these revenues.
8.        Depreciation is based on the replacement value of existing and
operating assets, plus the value of new assets in operation incorporated
during project construction. The depreciation rates for each type of asset
and the corresponding compound rate are shown in Annex 5 Table 1, page 2.
Depreciation rates of 2.86 to 3.65% were used for the new assets and slightly
less for the overall PDAM, (because of totally depreciated assets among the
old fixed assets).  In the past the transfer of assets from CK to the PDAMs
have been a slow process. Under the Project these assets should be transfer
shortly after their commissioning.



- 105 -                           ANNEX 11
Page 3
0perairng expenses
9.         Personnel.  Personnel expenses are based in detail analysis for
the number of employees required to operate each PDAM.  The integration of
serfa. citIsi and towns in a water enterprises results in economics of
sCale. The number of employees requixed to serve one thousand connections,
which be.tween 15 to 33 in the existing systems, is expected to be reduced to
about 10 by 1987 (compared with 3 in USA and about 7 in Latin America).
This increase in efficiency does not require reduction in present staff but
a better use of the additional staff needed after the project completion.
Some: I-ey persons (engineers, managers. etc) may require to be hired, and
traiLne e ven before the new connections will generate additional revenues.
This requires subsidies for operation (show as operational grants) for the
sta -t-up period of some PDAMis.
110        Chemicals and Power.  These expenses have been projected using
uncn CEostS ir constant prices per cubic meter produced.  These and other
unci prices are not fully reliable (details available in Project File) and
ave-age coefficients per cu m were used in some PDAMs (mainly Sidoarjo and
Sitabondo'. The unch:Lorinated water provided now would be chlorinated after
the new systems enter into operation.
i11        Ooher expenses.  Expenses in materials, maintenance, transporta-
tionC and general expenses were projected in relation to the number of
cornections, but allowing for economics cf scale and inflation. Since the
systei  would be new the expenses in materials and maintenance are expected
to iDe lower than for the old systems.  Values forecasted in the feasibility
reports were used until 1983.  In some PEMLs the forecasted expenses were
considered excessive and the coefficient per cubic meter produced adjusted
wit-hin. the range of the mission analysis of 60 BPAMs in Indonesian which are
presently In operation (available in Project File). This raises or lowers
some of the expenses between 1983 and 1984.
12.        Bad debts.  Bad debts are compvted (Annex 13) based in a percentage
of uncolleotable bills of 2% for residential users below 10 cu m, 1% for
other residential users, 5% for social consumers, 0.3% for large users, 17
for the iovernment and 15% for standpipes.  The bad debts allowance varies
bet:7i en 2524 and 3.12 for the different IPAMs, and if higher during the
13,        inflation.  The estimated rates of inflation for local costs are
13%  for 1933.l10% for 1984 and 7% thereafter.  The estimated foreign infla-
i i< n i5s 8%o in 1983, 7.5% in 1984, 7%0 in 1985 and 6% thereafter.  The
axehange ra-te Is estimated at Rp 700 per dollar.
' _        Consumption histogram.  The anallysis of the expected distribution
C.Cs7-Ie; ic;TonS and consumption (by 1988),, iS presented as Annex 8, Tables 1
to 3.  To avoi-d wlater wastage the minimum consumption, 'which is highly

- 106 -                          ANNEX 11
Page 4
is very conservative since no residential connections are assumed to use
more than 30 cu m per month, at which level the maximum tariff is applied
(three times the minimum tariff).
15.       Tariffs and affordability.  Revenues achievable based in this
consumption histogram, are shown in Annex 13. The average ratio between the
average and minimum tariff is 1.58, which is used to estimate the tariff for
minimum consumption (Annex 14). The progressivety for excess consumption
can be increased, in which case these ratio would become 2 or more. This
tariff progressivety allows the subsidization of urban poor, while maintain-
ing overall satisfactory tariffs. The tariff for minimum consumption is
assumed to be increased to 50 Rp cu m in June 1983. The full effect of this
increase is reflected in 1984. In 1985 and thereafter tariffs are assumed
sufficient to cover operation, maintenance and depreciation on revalued
assets. Althoug this has been proposed by Cipta Karya, it may not be fully
achievable in 1985, since the increase in the number of consumers and water
sold would be small in relation to the new assets entering into operation.
16.       Income distribution.  The income distribution was estimated by
consultants based in socio-economic surveys for cities and IKKs. Although
the results show some differences which are difficult to explain (i.e.
higher income in the IKKs than in the cities), these data is indicative of
levels of income (Annex 14). The relative poverty level, based in 1/3 of
the per capita income, shows that between 40 to 76% (average 60%) of the
population is in relative poverty.
17.       Affordability.  Expenditures for water services between 3 and 7%
of the household income are normally considered affordable. A level of 4%
of the income was used for the analysis of affordability. The results show
that minimum tariffs for house connection service of 40 Rp/cu m in 1983
prices are affordable to practically all, 88 Rp per cu m are affordable to
88% of the population and of 132 Rp/cu m are affordable to 76% of the
population.  The average tariffs which are affordable to 88% of the popula-
tion are 139 Rp/cu m (Annex 14). The percentage of monthly income required
for house connection and standpipe service is below 2% of the minimum family
income (para. 6.07).
18.       Balance statements.  Cash.  Minimum cash is about one month of
personnel expenses. Since there is no debt service, nor identifiable works
other than the project, the excess cash is allowed to acc-umulate. This cash
should only be used to increase, repair and expand the systems. Accounts
receivable are projected (net of bad debts) at about two months of billing.
Inventories are projected on the basis of annual expenditures in materials
and chemicals. Accounts payable are projected as 60 days of expenses (mainly
for power).
19.       Additional information.  Full financial projections, including
detailed tables on demand, project cost, financial assumptions, financial
statements, marginal cost and internal rate of return analysis are avail-
able in Project File for each PDAM. Monitoring indicators are presented in
Annex 12.

INDONESIA
EAST JAVA WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Governor's Instructions - Summary of Contents
The instructions issued by the Ministry of Home Affairs to the
uovernor of East Java, on February 12, 1983, which were passed in turn to
the beneficiary kabupatens/kotamadya, contain directi-es concerning:
(a) Management of PDAMs and the receipt of projec: assets by the PDAM
upon project completion.
(b)  Establishment of PDAMs by June 1983.
(c) The opening of subbranches in IKK towns.
(d) The employment of central government personnel to rrepare local
personnel for the tasks of managing PDAMs and participating in
construction.
(e) Water tariffs in conformity with national guidelines.
(f) PDAM actions to activate the supervisory board, increase the
numbers of house connections and manage an appropriate financial
system.
(g) Investment of capital in the PDAM by the Level II (local)
government.
(h) Funding of land acquisition by the Level II government.
(i) The conservation of water sources.
(j) Assignment of Level II government personnel to the project.
The instructions have attached a brief description of each subproject. The
instructions are in the Project File, Item B.2.



- 119 -                             ANNEX 16
Page 1>
INDONESIA
EAST JAVA WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Rate of Return
1.        Economic rates of return (ERR) have been calculated for the overaUl
PDAM, including all BNA cities and IKKs under their respective jurisdiction,s
This analysis is considered appropriate since the per capita investment cos
and the standards of service are similar, the administration and management
will be shared, the investment will start and reach its design horizon
within the same period, and the tariff will be uniform for all cities in the
same PDAM. However, the overall analysis is a rough estimate since only
preliminary feasibility reports are available, and some cities or IKKs halr-e
design horizons longer than the average, making difficult the estimation o-
the year when the overall capacity of the project will be reached.
2.        The rates of return have been calculated using the charges pai;
currently to water vendors for small quantities of consumption (15 lpcd) and
the covenanted tariff levels for the higher consumption as a proxy for
economic benefits. The analysis includes some complementary investmen-s
(additional pumps) required to fully utilize the project's capacity in some
cities. This, for on the IKKs which have a capacity to serve more tha.1
80-90% of their population (which are expected to be served only by 1ij90),
explains the increase in connections between 1988 and 1990. Following
present policies of the Government the revenue covenant requires tariffs and
other revenues to cover operation and depreciation starting in 1985=
Because this results in a relatively low tariff, and because of the long
period forecasted to build the project and reach the connection target
(1983-88), the ERRs are low, averaging +4% (Table 1). The ERR indicates the
economic justification for higher tariffs. Such tariffs are likely to
result from the proposed study on tariffs and cost recovery (paras. 1.21 and
6.06), and would provide a more meaningful ERR. Nevertheless, lower tariffs
are considered adequate at the begirLning in order to promote the acceptancea
of safe, publicly supplied water.

PDAMs                (%)     investment   benefits
Madiun               4.0        3.3          2.0

Average               4          3            2
/a Details in Project File.
3.        The ERR using tariffs as a proxy for consumer willingness to pay
measures only a part of the project benefits. This is especially relevant
to this project, since the water services are going to be heavily curtailed,
allowing the use of minimum amounts of water (half of the beneficiaries will
be served by standpipes, IKKs connections will have flow restriction devices,
and water pressure will limit the volume usable by other connections). The
willingness to pay for low volumes of water is shown by the price paid to
water vendors. This price is about Rp 250 per cu m (for about 1.2 per cu m
per family, purchased in 20 liter cans), but reaches Rp 1,000 per cu m in
Sidoarjo and Rp 2,000 per cu m in Panarukan. The willingness to pay for the
first 15 lpcd is estimated at Rp 250 per cu m /1 and for the remaining
consumption at the average tariff in each PDAM. To ensure that the PDAMs
are properly managed and to retain the staff to be trained the feasibility
study recommends a new salary structure which will increase the personnel
expense for new and previous employees. Both the benefits of the improve
service for existing consumers (continuous service, better water quality,
higher pressure) and the revised cost for the existing staff are not inclu-
ded in the economic analysis. A typical analysis is shown for Mojokerto,
Table 1. The analysis of the other PDAMs is in the Project File and is
summarized in Table 2.
/1 This is about 4 cu m for the total connections including standpipes.



- 122 -                             ANNEX 16
Page 4
6.        These rates of return underestimate the economic benefits, because
they do not capture externalities which accrue to the community, such as
improved health and reduced morbidity and mortality. Furthermore the water
purchased from water vendors is not purified and lacks the convenience of
piped water; therefore its price does not fully express the consumer's
willingness to pay. In any case justification of this project does not rest
upon these rates of return, but that at a minimum cost provides essential
and safe water supply to a heretofore unserved population (more than half of
whom are poor) and strengthens sector institutions and polices.
